<PN>22

<TT><beg eng>ANACREON

<WW>BIOGRAPHY

<TY>1 Suda

  Anacreon: a lyric poet of Teos, the son of Scythinus
or, according to other authorities, of Eumelus,
Parthenius or Aristocritus.<fn 1> He wrote elegiac and
iambic poems, all in the Ionic dialect. He lived in
the time of Polycrates, the tyrant of Samos, in Olympiad
52 (572/1-569/8 B.C.).<fn 2> But other authorities put
him in Olympiad 55 (560/59-557/6 B.C.) at the time
of Cyrus<fn 3> and Cambyses.<fn 4> Because of the revolt of

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Son of Scythinus or Parthenius, acc. to schol. Pl. Phdr.
235c; cf. elegiacs in schol. Pind. i 10 Drachm., J. Labarbe
Ant. Class. 37, 1968, 461-6. Richer, Portraits of the Greeks
i 77 records a herm (I.G. xiv 1133) inscribed<end eng> A)nak ... Sku
... Thi ... , <beg eng>prob. `A., son of Scythinus, of Teos'. For the
5th or 4th c. iambic poet Scythinus of Teos see Edmonds,
Elegy and Iambus ii 244 ff.
<fn 2>Some scholars emend the
date to Ol. 62 (532/1-529/8), the traditional date of
Polycrates' acme (cf. test. 2). But there were probably two
tyrants of this name, the father ruling c. 572-540, the son
(the Polycrates of Herodotus) ruling 533-522. A.'s
contemporary would be the son. Ol. 52 may have seen both
the accession of Polycrates I and the births of A. and P. II
(J.P. Barron, CQ 14, 1964, 210-29: see also fr. 491).
<fn 3>Cf. Zenobius 5.80 (see fr. 426); Cyrus began his reign in Ol. 55
(Eusebius).
<fn 4>`At the time of Cyrus and Polycrates',
according to Athen. 13. 599c (=Sa. test. 8); cf. Aristox. fr. 12
Wehrli: `Cambyses was contemporary with the tyranny of
Polycrates.' Since Ol. 55 does not fit Cyrus' son Cambyses
III, some scholars emend to Ol. 62, the date when Cambyses
succeeded Cyrus (e.g. Rohde, Rh. Mus. 33, 1878, 190).
Others read `Cyrus the son of Cambyses (II)': see J.
Labarbe, Ant. Class. 31, 1962, 184.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>25

<beg eng>Histiaeus he had to leave Teos and settled in Abdera
in Thrace.<fn 5> His life was devoted to the love of boys
and women and to song. He composed drinking
songs and iambics and the so-called Anacreontea.<fn 6>

<beg fn>
<fn 5>Cf. Sud. T 319, Strab. 14. 1. 30 = fr. 505(a). Histiaeus,
tyrant of Miletus involved in the Ionian revolt (see Hdt.
5.35-6; 6.1), is probably confused by Suda with Harpagus,
for whom see test.3.
<fn 6>Late imitations of A.
<end fn>

<WW>CHRONOLOGY<fn 1>

<TY>2 Eusebius, Chronicle

  Olympiad 61.1 (536/5)<fn 2>: Anacreon the lyric poet is
well known.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also test. 8, Sa. test. 8.
<fn 2>So Jerome and Armenian
version; a variant reading gives Ol. 62.2 (531/O B.C.). The
next entry records the accession of Polycrates (II) and his
brothers in 533/2. See also A. A. Mosshammer, The
Chronicle of Eusebius 297-300.
<end fn>

<TY>3 Aristoxenus, Life of Pythagoras

  It is recorded that approximately 514 years
elapsed between the Trojan War and the time of
Xenophanes the physicist,<fn 1> Anacreon, Polycrates,
the blockade and destruction of Ionia by Harpagus
the Mede<fn 2> and the migration of the Phocaeans to
Marseilles to escape it. For Pythagoras was contemporary
with all these.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Flor. 538/7 B.C. (Euseb.); but the traditional date of the fall
of Troy (e.g. in Eratosthenes) was 1184 B.C.
<fn 2>c. 540 B.C.; cf. Hdt. 1. 162-8, Strab. 6.1.1.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>27

<WW><beg eng>LIFE<fn 1>

<TY>4 Aelian, Historical Miscellanies<fn 2>

  Polycrates of Samos loved the arts, and he
honoured and favoured Anacreon of Teos, delighted
with both the man and his poetry.<fn 3> But I do not
praise his luxury.<fn 4>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>For anecdotes about A. see Tzetzes Chil. 4. 131. 235 ff.,
Stob. Flor. 93. 78, Max. Tyr. 21. 7.
<fn 2>Continued at fr. 414.
<fn 3>For A. and Polycrates see fr. 483, Paus. 1.2.3,
Ael. V. H. 12.25; Hdt. 3. 121 says A. was with him when he
was murdered in 522 B.C.
<fn 4>The word, used here of
Polycrates, is used of A. by Jul. Mis. 337a, Athen. 10. 429b
(=test. 18), A. P. 7. 33. 1; cf. Ar. Thesm. 159-63.
<end fn>

<TY>5 Apuleius, Flowers of Rhetoric

  Before the altar<fn 1> is a statue of Bathyllus<fn 2> dedicated
by the tyrant Polycrates. I think I have never
come across anything more perfect...This statue
may be taken to represent one of the youths of the
court -- one who was loved by the tyrant Polycrates
and is singing a song of Anacreon for friendship's
sake.<fn 3>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Of the temple of Hera in Samos.
<fn 2>See test. 11 n.5.
<fn 3>Text uncertain: perhaps `one of the youths who
were loved by the tyrant P. and of whom Anacreon of Teos
sings for friendship's sake'.
<end fn>

<TY>6 (Plato), Hipparchus

  Hipparchus sent a fifty-oared ship to fetch Anacreon
of Teos to Athens,<fn 1> and induced Simonides of

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. frr. 412, 495, 500 for A.'s stay in Athens; for the
archaeological evidence see S. Papaspyridi-Karouzou,
Bull. Corresp. Hell. 66 (1942) 248-54.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>29

<beg eng>Ceos by high pay and presents to be always at his
side.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 2>For the whole passage cf. Aristot. Ath. Pol. 18, Ael. V.H.
8. 2.
<end fn>

<TY>7 Scholiast on Pindar, Isthmians

  This refers to Alcaeus and Ibycus and Anacreon
and anyone else before Pindar who may have devoted
his attention to his favourite boy: for these
writers were older than Pindar. They say that when
Anacreon was asked why he did not write hymns to
gods but to boys, he replied, `Because they are my
gods.'

<WW>DEATH

<TY>8 (Lucian), On Longevity

  Anacreon the lyric poet lived eighty-five
years<fn 1>..

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. until c. 485 B.C.; according to schol. Aes. P.V. 128
(=Anacr. 412) he lived long enough to enjoy the works of
Aeschylus, whose first plays were performed c. 499-6 (first
victory 484).
<end fn>

<TY>9 Valerius Maximus, Memorable Deeds and Sayings
(on unusual deaths)

  The same was true of Anacreon, who surpassed
the common span of human life<fn 1> but perished when
a single pip obstinately stuck in his withered throat

<beg fn>
<fn 1>He fell ill once but recovered: Him. Or. 69.35 (=Anacr. 494).<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>31

<beg eng>as he sustained his poor remaining strength with
raisin-juice.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 2>Cf. Pliny, N. H. 7.7, Politian, Nutricia (Opera Omnia I.
543), Petrus Crinitus, de Honesta Disciplina 2.6, A. P. 7. 33.
<end fn>

<WW>PORTRAITS

<TY>10 Pausanias, Description of Greece

  On the Athenian acropolis there are statues of
Pericles and his father Xanthippus, who fought the
Persians in the naval engagement off Mycale.<fn 1> But
Pericles' statue is on one side, while near Xanthippus
stands Anacreon of Teos, the first poet after
Sappho of Lesbos to make love his main theme. The
statue represents him as a man singing when he is
drunk.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>479 B.C.; see fr. 493.
<fn 2>The statue in the Carlsberg
Glyptothek in Copenhagen is probably not a copy of the
Acropolis statue. A.P. 9.599 mentions a statue in Teos, W.
Peek, Gr. Vers-Inschriften i 1792. 1-3 one in Cyzicus.
<end fn>

<TY>11 Planudean Anthology: Leonidas of Tarentum, On
Anacreon<fn 1>

  Look at old<fn 2> Anacreon, dishevelled and unsteady
with wine, bent over the inlaid stone(?).<fn 3> See how
the old man gazing amorously with lascivious eyes<fn 4>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Anth. Plan. 307-9, all possibly on the statue mentioned
by Pausanias (test. 10). Representations of Anacreon
are catalogued in Richter, Portraits of the Greeks
i 75-8.
<fn 2>A. was habitually depicted in art and literature
(esp. the Anacreontea) as old: cf. e.g. fr. adesp. 35 = P. M. G.
953 (quoted in Sa. test. 8).
<fn 3>Obscure: see Gow-Page H. E. ii 341.
<fn 4>Cf. test. 12.3.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>33

<beg eng>trails his robe to his ankles. Stricken by wine he has
lost one of his two shoes, but keeps a wrinkled foot
in the other. He singing of delightful Bathyllus<fn 5> or
of Megisteus,<fn 6> holding in his hand the love-lorn lyre.
Father Dionysus, guard him, for it is not right for the
servant of Bacchus to fall because of Bacchus.

<beg fn>
<fn 5>Cf. Anth. Plan. 307, A. P. 7.30, 31, test. 5, frr. 402, 471,
503.
<fn 6>Cf. Anth. Plan. 307, A. P. 7.25, 27, frr. 352-3, 416.
<end fn>

<WW>EPITAPH<fn 1>

<TY>12 Palatine Anthology: Antipater of Sidon, On
Anacreon

  Anacreon, glory of the Ionians, may you among
the dead not be without your beloved revels or your
lyre; but gazing amorously with lascivious eyes may
you sing clear-voiced, shaking the garland on your
perfumed hair, turning towards Eurypyle<fn 2> or Megisteus
or the Ciconian<fn 3> locks of Thracian Smerdies,<fn 4>
as you spout forth sweet wine, your robe quite
drenched with Bacchus, wringing unmixed nectar
from its folds; for all your life, old man, was poured
out as an offering to these three -- the Muses, Dionysus
and Eros.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. A. P. 7, 23-26, 28-33.
<fn 2>Cf. A. P. 7.31, fr. 372.
<fn 3>The Cicones were a Thracian tribe.
<fn 4>Cf. A. P. 7.25, 29,
31, frr. 346(6), 347, 366, 402, 414, 503.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>35

<WW><beg eng>ANCIENT EDITIONS<fn 1>

<TY>13 Palatine Anthology: Crinagoras

  The delightful quintet<fn 2> of lyric books inside this
case brings works of inimitable charm -- Anacreon's,
which the pleasant old man from Teos wrote over the
wine or with the help of the Desires. We come as a
gift for the holy day<fn 3> of Antonia,<fn 4> whose beauty and
wisdom are unexcelled.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also Alc. test. 11; for ancient commentators see Sa.
test. 22 (Didymus), Sa. test. 39 (Clearchus); others were
Aristophanes of Byzantium (fr. 408), Aristarchus and
Taenarus (fr. 352), Heraclides Ponticus (Plut. Per. 27 on
fr. 372), Chamaeleon (fr. 372), Zenodotus (frr. 408, 431); cf.
fr. 461. Aristoxenus alluded to him (Sa. test. 37 n.2).
<fn 2>Books 1, 2 and 3 of A.'s lyrics are attested. They
were prob. edited by Aristophanes of Byzantium and by
Aristarchus: see Heph. pp. 68, 74 Consbr. (= fr. 348, Alc.
test. 11), Gentili Anacreon xxvi ff.
<fn 3>Prob. her birthday.
<fn 4>Prob. Antonia Minor, daughter of Mark Anthony and
Octavia and mother of Germanicus and the emperor
Claudius.
<end fn>

<WW>METRES<fn 1>

<TY>14 Trichas, On the Nine Metres

  The iambic dimeter catalectic is also known to us
as the hemiambic. It is important, and the ancients
call it the anacreontic because Anacreon used it
extensively.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>For further references see the indexes to Grammatici
Latini (Keil) and Hephaestion, Enchiridion (Consbruch)
under `Anacreon' and `anacreonteum metrum', B. Gentili,
Anacreon 108-115, Anacr. fr. 499.
<fn 2>E.g. at fr. 429: cf.
`Mar. Vict.' (Aphthonius) (vi 153 Keil).<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>37

<TY><beg eng>15 Mallius Theodorus, On Metres

  The anacreontic<fn 1> sounds most impressive if it has
an anapaest in the first foot, then two iambs plus one
syllable.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>E.g. at fr. 395; for modern analysis see e.g. D.S. Raven,
Greek Metre 67.
<fn 2>Cf. schol. B. Heph., Append. Dionys.,
Append. Rhetor. (pp. 285, 316, 343 Consbr.).
<end fn>

<WW>THE VERDICT OF ANTIQUITY<fn 1>

<TY>16 Hermogenes, Kinds of Style (on simplicity)

  In general, thoughts which are pure are thoughts
of simplicity... More particularly, one would call
`simple' the thoughts of unaffected and to some
extent childlike, not to say stupid, natures. For
example, it is `simple' to go over events and recount
them when there is no necessity and no-one is asking
a question. Most of Anacreon is like this...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. also Dion. Hal. on Sa. fr. 1, Sa. testt. 8, 39, 42, 47-49,
52-54, Alc. test. 27, Anacreont. 7. 2, 15, 7, 60(b). 7.
<end fn>

<TY>17 Palatine Anthology: The Garland of Meleager

  And he entwined Anacreon, whose sweet lyric
song is indeed of nectar, but a bloom which cannot
be transplanted into elegiacs.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Meleager had to exclude from his anthology the greater
part of A.'s poetry as being lyric, not elegiac.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>39

<TY><beg eng>18 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Anacreon, who made all his poetry depend on the
subject of intoxication,<fn 1> is unusual. For he is attacked
as having given himself over in his poetry to
laxity and luxury, since most people are unaware
that he was sober while he composed and that he was
an upright man,<fn 2> who merely pretended to be drunk,
though there was no necessity for his doing so.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Ov. Ars Am. 3. 329-31, Sext. Emp. Adv. Gramm. 1. 298,
Porph. on Hor. A. P. 85.
<fn 2>Socrates refers to `the wise
A.' (Pl. Phdr. 235b); cf. frr. 363, 500.
<end fn>

<TY>19 Horace, Odes

  ... nor has time destroyed Anacreon's playful
poems.<fn 1>

`Acro' on the passage.

  ... `playful', because he wrote poems suited to
merry-making and convivial occasions.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See Sa. test. 53, Alc. test. 27, Luc. Symp. 17, V.H. 2. 15 for
their performance at dinners.
<end fn>

<TY>20 Cicero, Tusculan Disputations

  All of Anacreon's poetry is erotic.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Clem. Al. Strom. 1. 78 says A. invented love-poetry. For
Cic.'s derogatory tone cf. Philod. de Mus. 14. 8-13 (the Stoic
Diogenes said A. corrupted the young by his ideas), Plut.
Per. 2.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>41

<TT><beg eng>ANACREON

Frr. 346-7 are papyrus finds; frr. 348-50 are attributed to
Book 1, 351 to Book 1 or 2, 352-4 to Book 2, 355-6 to Book 3.
The rest cannot be assigned to any book: frr. 357-445 are
arranged on metrical principles, 446-60 have consecutive
words but are too short for such arrangement, 461-87 are
isolated words in alphabetical order, 488-98 contain
references to the content of various poems.

  Papyrus fragments of Anacreon published in 1986 by M.
W. Haslam (Ox. Pap. LIII 3695) reveal a coincidence with
fr. 443 and provide tatters of what seems to be mainly erotic
verse.

<TY>346 Oxyrhynchus papyrus (2nd c. A.D.)

  (1) ... nor ... but you have a timid heart as well,
you lovely-faced boy, and (your mother) thinks that
she tends you (at home), keeping a firm hold on you;
(but you escaped to?) the fields of hyacinth, where
Cyprian Aphrodite tied her (lovely?) horses freed
from the yoke; and you darted down in the midst of
the (throng?), so that many of the citizens have
found their hearts fluttering.

  Herotima,<fn 1> public highway, public highway<fn 2>...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Probably the beginning of a new poem; if not, we must
translate `you lovely-faced girl' in v. 3 above.
<fn 2>See 446.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>43

  <beg eng>(2) ... and I was boxing with a tough opponent,<fn 1>
(but now) I look up and raise my head again... I
owe many thanks, (Dionysus?), for having escaped
Love's bonds completely, bonds made harsh by
Aphrodite. Let<fn 2> wine be brought in a jar, let bubbling
water be brought, let ... be summoned...
grace, (perfect?)...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Presumably Eros: cf. 396.
<fn 2>No need to see the
beginning of a new poem here, especially if the supplement
`Dionysus' is correct.
<end fn>

  (3) ... by night ... sweet and ... But the lovely
gifts of the Pierian Muses<fn 1> are here...and (to) the
Graces<fn 2>...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Archil. 1. 2.
<fn 2>Poem ends two verses later.<end eng>

  (6) (scholiast) addressed to Smerdies<fn 1>

<TY>347 Oxyrhynchus papyrus (2nd or early 3rd c.A.D.) fr. 1

  ... and of the hair, which shadowed your soft

fallen into coarse hands and tumbled in a heap in the

for what is one to do if he has not succeeded even for
the sake of Thrace<fn 1>?

<beg fn>

<TY>347A = Sa. 213C (first words of poems by Sappho, Alcaeus,

<TY>348 Hephaestion, On Poems

  The type which allows two classifications in strophic
correspondence is `common', e.g. the first song<fn 1> of
Anacreon, `I beseech you ... beasts' etc.<fn 2>: in the current
edition<fn 3> the stanza (`strophe') has eight lines, and the song
is monostrophic<fn 4>; but the stanza can be differently divided
into a triad plus a pentad with a pherecratean concluding
both the three-line and the five-line sections.

  I beseech you, deer-shooter, fair-haired child of
Zeus, Artemis, queen of wild beasts, who now somewhere

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. the first poem of Book 1.
<fn 2>All eight lines are quoted by the scholiast.
<fn 3>That of Aristarchus: see test. 13 n.2.
<fn 4>I.e., has a repeated eight-line stanza.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>49

<beg eng>by the eddies of the Lethaeus<fn 5> look down on a
city<fn 6> of bold-hearted men and rejoice, since the
citizens whom you shepherd are not untamed.<fn 7>

<beg fn>
<fn 5>Tributary of the Maeander.
<fn 6>Magnesia, near which was the temple of Artemis Leucophryene.
<fn 7>Civilised Greeks under Persian rule; A. may have prayed for help for
the Magnesians in the remainder of the poem: see Bowra,
G. L. P. 274; Page, Studi...L. Castiglioni 2. 661 ff. thinks
the citizens are the Persians.
<end fn>

<TY>352 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Cynuclus said, `Since questions about garlands have
come up, tell us, Ulpian, what `the garland of Naucratis' is
in the charming Anacreon; for the sweet poet says (fr. 434);
and why in the same poet are some people garlanded with
willow? In Book 2 of his songs he says:

  The kindly Megistes has for ten months now garlanded
himself with willow and drunk the honeysweet
must.<fn 1>

The garland of willow is absurd, since willow is suitable for
ropes and wicker-work. So tell us something about these
things, since they deserve investigation...' Democritus
said, `The eminent grammarian Aristarchus in his
explanation of the passage, my friend, said the ancients
used to make garlands even from willow. Taenarus says
that country people used willow for garlands...'

<beg fn>
<fn 1>A.E. Harvey, C.Q. n.s. 7 (1957) 222 n. 1 suggests that A. is
mocking the garlands and drink of Megistes, for whom see
test. 11 n.6.<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>53

<TY><beg eng>353 Scholiast on Odyssey<end eng>

  mu=qos <beg eng>(`word, tale') now means `rebellion, civil strife'; and
so Anacreon says of the rebellious fishermen in Samos

  and in the island, Megistes, the talkers hold sway
over the sacred city,

using<end eng> muqih=tai, <beg eng>`talkers', instead of<end eng> stasiastai/, <beg eng>`rebels'.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>According to Antigonus they were led by one
Herostratus; for political opposition to Polycrates see
Hdt. 3.44 f.
<end fn>

<TY>354 Ammonius, On Similar but Different Words<end eng>

  diabo/htos <beg eng>and<end eng> e)pibo/htos <beg eng>are different in meaning: the man
known for his virtue is<end eng> diabo/htos, <beg eng>`famous', the man with a
bad reputation is<end eng> e)pibo/htos, <beg eng>`notorious': cf. Anacreon Book
2:

  and you will make me notorious among the
neighbours.

<TY>355 Suda

  `His talents would tantalise Tantalus'<fn 1>: Tantalus was
famous for his wealth and has become proverbial for it. He
was a wealthy Phrygian, famous for his talents, reputedly
the son of Pluto (Lady Wealth) and Zeus. Anacreon uses the
proverb in Book 3.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Lit. `he equals Tantalus in talents', a talent being a large
sum of money. See also P. Oxy. 3695 fr. 3.<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>55

<TY><beg eng>356 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  But in Anacreon the mixture is one part wine to two
parts water:

  Come, boy, bring me a bowl, so that I may drink
without stopping for breath; pour in ten ladles of
water and five of wine, that I may once again play
the Bacchant with decorum;

and further on he calls the drinking of unmixed wine
Scythian drinking:

  Come again, let us no longer practise Scythian
drinking with clatter and shouting over our wine,
but drink moderately amid beautiful songs of
praise.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>From Book 3 of Anacreon (Porphyrio); the quotations
must come from the same poem.
<end fn>

<TY>357 Dio Chrysostom, Orations

  It follows that the king should not offer prayers like
those of other men nor call on the gods in prayer after the
manner of the Ionian poet Anacreon:

  Lord, with whom Love the subduer and the blue-eyed
Nymphs and radiant Aphrodite play, as you
haunt the lofty mountain peaks, I beseech you: come<end eng>

<PN>57

<beg eng>to me with kindly heart, hear my prayer and find it
acceptable: give Cleobulus good counsel, Dionysus,
that he accept my love.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Considered a complete poem by some; for Cleobulus cf.
359, 360, 402, 471, Max. Tyr. 21. 7.
<end fn>

<TY>358 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner<fn 1>

  Chamaeleon in his treatise On Sappho actually declares
that some say it was to her that the following lines were
addressed by Anacreon:

  Once again golden-haired Love strikes me with his
purple ball and summons me to play with the girl in
the fancy sandals; but she -- she comes from Lesbos
with its fine cities -- finds fault with my hair because
it is white, and gapes after another -- girl.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See Sa. test. 8.
<fn 2>Prob. a complete poem.

<TY>359 `Herodian', On Figures of Speech

  The figure polyptoton (repetition of a word in different
cases) can be found in some of the poets: in Archilochus (fr.
115) and in Anacreon, who uses three cases:

  I love Cleobulus, I am mad about Cleobulus, I gaze
at Cleobulus.<end eng>

<PN>59

<TY><beg eng>360 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  And Sappho says to the man who is extravagantly
admired for his figure and considered handsome (138). And
what does Anacreon say?

  Boy with the girlish glance,<fn 1> I seek you, but you
do not notice, not knowing that you hold the reins of
my soul.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cleobulus? cf. 402.
<end fn>

<TY>361 Strabo, Geography

  One might well believe that it was from their great
prosperity that the men of these parts, especially their
rulers, got the name of `long-livers', and that this was why
Anacreon said:

  I would not wish for Amalthea's horn<fn 1> nor to be
king of Tartessus<fn 2> for a hundred and fifty years,

and why Herodotus (1.163, 165) even gave the name of the
king in question, Arganthonius.<fn 3>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Amalthea was the she-goat who nursed the infant Zeus;
her horns flowed one with ambrosia, one with nectar, the
prototype of the horn of plenty, cornu copiae.
<fn 2>District and city at the mouth of the Baetis (mod. Guadalquivir).
<fn 3>Acc. to Hdt. Arg. ruled Tartessus for 80 of his
120 years.
<end fn>

(are heavy with?) water, and the wild storms

<beg fn>

<TY>363 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner<fn 1>

  And the wise Anacreon says somewhere:

  Why are you all of a flutter, anointing with perfume
your breast that is hollower than the pipes of
Pan?

He urges the perfuming of the breast, in which the heart
lies, clearly because the heart too is soothed by sweet
scents.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The passage follows Alc. 362 (second fragment); Athen.'s
comment fits the Alc. passage, not the text of Anacr., which
is mockery of an old man.
<end fn>

<TY><beg eng>373 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  The antispastic tetrameter catalectic which has an
iambic in the second metron is called priapean, e.g.<fn 1>

  I dined by breaking off a small piece of thin
honeycake, but I drained a jar of wine. Now I
tenderly strike my lovely lyre in a serenade to my
dear girl.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Attributed to Anacreon by Athenaeus.
<fn 2>Text of last words uncertain: perhaps a proper name, e.g. `my dear
Poliarche'.
<end fn>

<TY>374 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  I myself, being a music-lover, have often wondered
whether the instrument called the magadis is a sort of pipes
or a type of lyre; for the sweet Anacreon says somewhere:

  Holding the magadis I strike its twenty strings,<fn 1>
while you, Leucaspis, enjoy the fun of youth.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Text uncertain. Athen. goes on to quote authors who
seem to speak of the magadis as a pipe.
<end fn>

<TY>375 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  We know also of pipes called half-size, about which
Anacreon says:

  Who has turned his thoughts to lovely youth and
dances to the tender half-size pipes<fn 1>?

These pipes are smaller than the complete ones... They
are the same as the so-called `child-pipes', which are not
suitable for public festivals but are used at parties. This is
why Anacreon calls them `tender'.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Pipes with three holes instead of six.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>69

<TY><beg eng>376 Hephaestion, On Poems

  In poems there are also the so-called epodes -- the noun is
masculine -- when an addition is made to a long line, e.g.
Archilochus, 172. 1 f., 182; but when the order is reversed<fn 1>
it is called a pro-ode, as in Anacreon:

  See, once again I climb up and dive from the
Leucadian cliff<fn 2> into the grey waves, drunk with
love.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. the shorter line comes first.
<fn 2>See Sa. test. 23.
<end fn>

<TY>377 Scholiast on Homer, Iliad 24. 278 (`mules which the
Mysians once gave Priam')

  `Mysians' because they were neighbours of the Enetians,
from whom came the breed of mules,<fn 1> or because Mysian
mules were particularly fine. Cf. Anacreon:

  and the Mysians discovered the breeding of maremounting
asses

with horses, whence come mules.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Il. 2. 852.
<end fn>

<TY>378 Scholiast on Aristophanes, Birds (`See, I fly up')

  This comes from Anacreon's lines:

  See, I fly up on light wings to Olympus in search of
Love; for (the boy) does not wish to enjoy the fun of
youth with me.<fn 1>

That is why the two lines have the<end eng> x.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1><beg eng>Cf. 445, Sa. 163.
<fn 2>Marginal mark used by Aristarchus
to indicate something noteworthy.<end eng>
<end fn>

<TY><beg eng>379 Lucian, Heracles

<TX begin>my greying beard<TX end>

<TX begin>fly past me on wings of shining gold.<TX end>

<TY>380 Himerius, Orations<fn 1>

  Hail, dear light, with a smile on your lovely face --

for I shall take a song from the lyre and bring it in
honour of your visit. I should gladly have persuaded the
words themselves to be my lyre and poetry, so that I might
sing of you with youthful abandon, as did Simonides and
Pindar of Dionysus and Apollo; but the words are proud
and stiff-necked, ranging without restraint and frisking
beyond the confines of their rhythms; and so I made a small
request of Poetry to give me a song of Teos -- that is the
Muse I love -- from the stores of Anacreon; and I come with
this hymn of praise for you, having myself made an
addition to the song: `Oh light of the Greeks and of all of us
who dwell in the holy plain of Pallas and the groves of the
Muses.'

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The opening words of a speech addressed in Athens to the
proconsul Basilius.
<end fn>

<TY><beg eng>385 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  A common form of choriambic tetrameter catalectic has
the second metron iambic as well as the close; cf. Anacreon:

  I come up from the river bringing (the washing) all
bright.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Presumably the beginning of a poem; the speaker is a
woman.
<end fn>

<TY>386 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  And so the whole verse (the cratinean) becomes a mixed
choriambic like Anacreon's

  I saw Simalus in the chorus holding his lovely
lyre.

<TY>387 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  Anacreon added the ithyphallic [1-<*>-<*> --]1 not to an
iambic length but to a choriambic with iambic admixture
[1-<*><*>-|<*>-<*>-]1:

  I asked Strattis the perfumer<fn 1> whether he would
let his hair grow long.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>`The lyre-maker' in Hephaestion's text, `the perfumer' in
Pollux.
<end fn>

<TY>388 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner<fn 1>

<TY>391 Scholiast on Pindar, Olympian 8 (`to make a crown')

<TY>395 Stobaeus, Anthology (on death and its inevitability)

<PN>81

<beg eng>often weep in fear of Tartarus: for the recess of

it is certain that he who goes down does not come up
again.

<TY>396 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

Demetrius, On Style

  The rhythm is exactly that of an old man drunk.

<TY>402 Maximus of Tyre, Orations

  The art of the craftsman of Teos is of the same kind and
character<fn 1>: he is in love with all who are beautiful and
praises them all. His poems are full of the hair of Smerdis
and the eyes of Cleobulus and the youthful beauty of
Bathyllus. But even in these verses you may see his
moderation: he says:

<N>(a)

  and I long to enjoy the fun of youth with you, for
you have graceful ways;

and again he says that

<N>(b)

  just deeds are beautiful in Love's view;

and he has surely revealed his art when he says

<N>(c)

  for children might love me for my words: for I sing
graceful songs and I know how to speak graceful
words.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Sa. test. 20.<end eng>

<TY><beg eng>408 Aelian, On the Nature of Animals

frightened, left in the woods away from his horned
mother.

Those who corrupt the text,<fn 2> actually saying that the
correct reading is<end eng> e)roe/sshs, <beg eng>`lovely mother', are vigorously
attacked by Aristophanes of Byzantium.

<TY>412 Scholiast on Aeschylus, Prometheus Bound

  The anacreontic rhythm is a broken rhythm<fn 1> suited to
lament. Anacreon lived in Attica when he was in love with
Critias, and he took great delight in the songs of the
tragedian (Aeschylus).<fn 2> They did not use the rhythms
indiscriminately but only in passages of lament ... The
passage is similar in rhythm to

  Once again, won't you let me go home, now that I
am drunk?

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. the ionics undergo anaclasis.
<fn 2>See test. 8 n.1 and fr. 495.
<end fn>

<TY>413 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

tetrameter brachycatalectic:

great hammer and dipped me in the wintry torrent.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>To temper the metal.<end eng>

<TY><beg eng>414 Stobaeus, Anthology (against beauty)

  Favorinus<fn 1: ... and so Anacreon would seem to be
ridiculous and petty in blaming the boy<fn 2> for cutting off his
hair when he says:

  You have cut off the perfect flower of your soft
hair.

Anacreon, wait a moment and you will see everything cut
off.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>2nd c. A.D. rhetorician.
<fn 2>Smerdies: see test. 12 n. 4.
<end fn>

Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  It is remarkable that the tyrant Polycrates is nowhere
recorded as having summoned either women or boys to
his court, although he was passionately devoted to the
company of males, to the extent of being a rival of the poet
Anacreon at the time when in anger he cut off the hair of
his beloved boy.

Aelian, Historical Miscellanies<fn 1>

  Anacreon praised Smerdies, the favourite of Polycrates,
too warmly, and the youth enjoyed the praise ...; but
Polycrates was jealous that Anacreon had honoured
Smerdies, and seeing that the youth returned the poet's
love he cut off the boy's hair to disgrace him and cause
Anacreon grief. But Anacreon, showing good sense and
self-control, did not presume to blame Polycrates but
rather turned his reproach on the boy in the words which
upbraided his rashness and folly in taking arms against his
own hair. Let Anacreon sing the song on what happened to
the hair: he will sing it better than I.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Continued from test. 4.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>95

<TY><beg eng>415 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  In the beginning the libation was given to the gods as
their due, the cottabus<fn 1> to the beloved: they assiduously
practised the cottabus, a Sicilian game, as the poem of
Anacreon of Teos shows:

  (throwing the drops of?) the Sicilian cottabus with
curved arm.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See Alc. 322.
<end fn>

<TY>416 Etymologicum Magnum<fn 1>

  From<end eng> a)bakh/s, <beg eng>`quiet', comes<end eng> a)bakw=, <beg eng>`I am quiet', just as<end eng>
eu)sebw=, <beg eng>`I am reverent', comes from<end eng> eu)sebh/s, <beg eng>`reverent'.
There is also the form<end eng> a)baki/zw, <beg eng>`I am quiet'; cf. Anacreon:

  But I hate all who have sullen and difficult ways. I
have learned that you, Megistes, are one of the quiet
ones.

He uses<end eng> a)bakizome/nwn, <beg eng>`quiet', in the sense of `peaceful,
causing no disturbance'.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The passage follows Sa. 120.
<fn 2>This may be wrong:
`quiet' probably = `sullen' (lit. `underground') and `difficult'
(Page, Wien. Stud. 79, 1966, 30 ff.); cf. 352 n. 1.
<end fn>

<TY>417 Heraclitus, Homeric Allegories

  Moreover<fn 1> Anacreon of Teos, abusing the meretricious
spirit and arrogance of a haughty woman, used the
`allegory' of a horse to describe her frisky disposition:

  Thracian filly, why do you look at me from the
corner of your eye and flee stubbornly from me,
supposing that I have no skill? Let me tell you, I

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The passage follows Alc. 6.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>97

<beg eng>could neatly put the bridle on you and with the reins
in my hand wheel you round the turnpost of the race-course;
instead, you graze in the meadows and frisk
and frolic lightly, since you have no skilled horseman
to ride you.

<TY>418 Hephaestion, Handbook of Metres

<beg fn>

<TY>424 Ammonius, On Similar but Different Words

  The active voice<end eng> gh=mai, <beg eng>`to marry', differs from the middle
voice<end eng> gh/masqai, <beg eng>`to get married', in that the man `marries',
the woman `gets married'...; Anacreon observes the
distinction when he ridicules someone for his effeminacy:<end eng>
<PN>101

  <beg eng>and the chamber in which he did not marry but got
married.

<TY>425 Plutarch, On Common Notions: Against the Stoics

  So when they are thirsty they have no need of water,
when they are hungry no need of bread:

  You are like gentle guests, needing only shelter
and fire.

Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  Noteworthy forms of the iambic are dimeters acatalectic,
such as those in which whole poems of Anacreon are
written,<fn 1> and trimeters: `You are like gentle guests.'

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See 428.

<TY>426 Zenobius, Proverbs

  They say that the Carians,<fn 1> when at war with Darius of
Persia, in obedience to an old oracle bidding them take the
bravest men as allies, went to Branchidae and asked the
god there if they should take the Milesians as allies; and
the god replied:

<TX begin>Once long ago the Milesians were brave.<TX end>

But the line is found before this in Anacreon, who
flourished about the time of Cyrus the Persian.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Acc. to the scholiast on Aristophanes it was to Polycrates
of Samos that the oracle was given.<end eng>

<TY><beg eng>445 Himerius, Orations

  Now I should have had songs of Teos and the lyre of
Anacreon which he, whenever scorned by a beloved boy,
knows how to use against the Loves themselves. I would
have addressed them in his words:

  You are violent and wicked, and you do not know
against whom you will hurl your weapons.

Perhaps too I would have uttered the threat made against
the Loves by Anacreon: he once loved a handsome youth,
and when he saw that the youth paid little attention to him
he tuned his lyre and threatened that if the Loves did not at
once wound the youth, he would never again strike up a
song in their praise.<fn 1>

<TY>491 Himerius, Orations<fn 1>

  When Polycrates was a youth - he was king, this
Polycrates, not only of Samos but of the whole Greek sea
`by which the earth is bounded'<fn 2> -- he loved the arts,
especially song, and persuaded his father to help him foster
his love of the arts; so his father sent for Anacreon, the
lyric poet, and gave him to his son to tutor him in his
beloved music, and under his supervision the boy worked
with his lyre to attain kingly excellence and looked like
fulfilling the Homeric prayer<fn 3> for his father by being `very
powerful'<fn 4> and better than anyone else.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See test. 1 n. 2, Ibycus, test. 1.
<fn 2>Perhaps a quotation from Anacreon.
<fn 3>Il. 6. 476 ff.
<fn 4>With a play on his name, poly-krates.
<end fn>

<TY>492 Himerius, Orations

  And since, my children, this man, as if he were a god,
makes us appear like those whom poets often change into
mortal shape and various forms and bring into the midst of
cities and peoples to deal with the lawlessness and just
behaviour of men, as Athena is shown in Homer and
Dionysus in Anacreon and Euripides ...

<TY>493 Himerius, Orations

Polycrates, was glad to address the great Xanthippus,<fn 1> and
Pindar took pleasure in saluting Hiero before Zeus.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See test. 10, Bowra, G. L. P. 301 f.
<end fn>

<TY>494 Himerius, Orations

<TY><beg eng>495 Plato, Charmides

  Socrates (to Charmides and Critias). Your father's house,
the house of Critias, son of Dropides, has been celebrated
by Anacreon<fn 1> and Solon<fn 2> and many other poets, so that it
is famed in tradition as being pre-eminent in beauty, in

<TY>497 Zenobius, Proverbs

  `He is more proud than Peleus of his sword.' This proverb
is mentioned by Anacreon and by Pindar in his Nemeans.
They say that the sword was made by Hephaestus and was
a gift to Peleus from the gods in reward for his virtue; when
he used it he was always successful both in battle and in
the chase.

<TY>498 Scholiast on Apollonius of Rhodes (`through a
beautiful porch')

  ... `beautiful' either because the building was royal or
because of its love interest; for that is how lovers speak: so
Anacreon says of the woman he loves, ...<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The quotation is lost.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>139

<TY><beg eng>499 (on the metres of Anacreon)

(a) Caesius Bassus, On Metres

  Catullus, following Sappho, Anacreon and other authors,
did not avoid all these types<fn 1> of (phalaecian) hendecasyllabic
as being faulty: he included them as legitimate.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Lines beginning with trochee or iamb instead of spondee.
<end fn>

(b) `Marius Victorinus' (Aphthonius) Grammar

  If the first syllable is removed from the (lesser) asclepiad,
an anacreontic will result with the form --<*>--<*>-<*>-.

(c) the same

  ... an antispastic trimeter catalectic gives an
anacreontic<fn 1> of the form <*>--<*>--<*>--.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>For other units labelled anacreontic see Servius, Cent.
Metr. (iv 458 ff. Keil) and passages listed by Page, P.M.G.
499 (fin.), together with testt. 14, 15.
<end fn>

<TY>500 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  And wise Anacreon, who is on everyone's lips, is always
singing the praises of Love; and so the excellent Critias<fn 1>
says this of him: `Sweet Anacreon, who once wove the
songs for women's melodies,<fn 2> was brought to Greece by
Teos, the excitement of the drinking-party, the deceiver of
women, the rival of the pipes, lover of the lyre, sweet healer
of pain: never shall love of you grow old or die, so long as
the boy carries round the water mixed with wine for the
cups, dispensing the toasts from left to right; so long as
female choirs<fn 2> perform the night-long rites; so long as the
scale-pan, daughter of bronze, sits on the high summit of
the cottabus<fn 3> to receive the drops of the Bromian.'<fn 4>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>5th c. politician and poet.
<fn 2>None of A.'s maiden-songs survives, but see 501.
<fn 3>See 415.
<fn 4>Dionysus, god of wine.<end eng>
<end fn>

<TY>504 = elegiac fr. 3

<TY>505 Fragments of uncertain authorship ascribed by some
to Anacreon

(a) Strabo, Geography

  Teos is situated on a peninsula, and it has a harbour. It is
the birthplace of Anacreon, the lyric poet, in whose day the
Teians abandoned their city and moved to Abdera in
Thrace, since they would not tolerate the insolence of the
Persians; whence these words:

<TX begin>Abdera, fair colony of the Teians.<TX end>

(b) Strabo, Geography (on Samos)

  Syloson was left a private citizen by his brother
(Polycrates); but he presented Darius with a robe which he
coveted when he saw him wearing it, and so Darius, when
he eventually became king, gave Syloson the tyranny in
return. His rule was so harsh that Samos became short of
men. This was the origin of the saying,

<TX begin>thanks to Syloson there is plenty of room.<TX end>

(c) = `Sappho or Alcaeus' fr. 1<end eng>
<PN>145

<beg eng>(d) Clement of Alexandria, Miscellanies

  For when Anacreon<fn 1> had written,

  For I am eager to sing of tender Love, his head
garlanded with luxuriant flowers: he is the ruler
over gods, he is the subduer of mortals,
Euripides wrote, `For Love assails not men alone.'

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The lines almost certainly belong to the Anacreontea.
<end fn>

Fulgentius, Mythologies

  According to Anacreon,<fn 1> a most ancient author, when
Zeus was beginning warfare against the Titans, i.e. the
sons of Titan, brother of Cronus, and had sacrificed to
Heaven, he saw an eagle fly nearby as a favourable omen
for victory. In return for this happy omen, and particularly
because it was indeed followed by victory, he put a golden
eagle on his war standards and dedicated it as a protection
for his valour; from these are derived the Roman standards
of this type.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Probably a later Anacreon: see R.E. Suppl. 1 p. 76 (Anakreon
1a).
<end fn>

`Acro' on Horace, Odes<fn 1>

  Moreover Anacreon wrote satire<fn 2>; he was the friend of
Lysander.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The passage follows test. 19.
<fn 2>E.g. 388; the text of the
following words is doubtful: some emend `friend' to
`enemy'.
<end fn>

(e) Scholiast on Aratus, Phaenomena 640

  Oenopion: son of Dionysus and Ariadne; lived on the
island of Chios and ruled over it, as Anacreon<fn 1> says:

<TX begin>except Oenopion.<TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Probably the later Anacreon, cited by Fulgentius in (d)
above, an Alexandrian writer, author of another
Phaenomena.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>147

<beg eng>(f) Inscription on a calyx krater (c. 513-508 B.C.)<fn 1>

  Apollo, (I beseech) you and blessed (Artemis)

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The words proceed from the mouth of one Ecphantides,
the host at a symposium: the vase-painter is Euphronius.
<end fn>

  tera/mwn, <beg eng>genitive<end eng> tera/mwnos: <beg eng>this usage of Plato in his

<PN>151

<WW><beg eng>EPIGRAMS

Meleager included in his Garland epigrams attributed to
Anacreon (test. 17), and eighteen carry his name in the
Palatine Anthology. Some may be authentic, e.g. 100, 107,
108, but certainty is impossible: the author's name was not
added to epitaphs or dedications before the end of the 5th c.
B.C., and there is seldom any corroborative evidence about
the authorship. Some are demonstrably later than Anacreon,
e.g. 114, 115, `Simon. 156D.' and probably 105; the manner of
102 and the content of 113 point to Hellenistic authorship.

<TY>100D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon of Teos: on the soldier Agathon at Abdera

  The mighty Agathon who died for Abdera was
mourned at the pyre by this whole city; for in the
whirl of hateful battle blood-loving Ares never slew
any youth such as he was.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Perhaps an elegiac lament rather than a true epitaph.
<end fn>

<TY>101D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon: on the heroism of Timocritus

  Timocritus, whose tomb<fn 1> this is, was strong in the
wars: Ares spares not the brave but the cowards.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The Doric form<end eng> sa=ma <beg eng>makes attribution to Anacreon
uncertain: see also Friedlnder-Hoffleit, Epigrammata 69.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>153

<TY><beg eng>102D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon of Teos: on Clenorides, who was shipwrecked

  You too, Clenorides, were destroyed by your
desire for your fatherland, when you put your trust
in the wintry blast of the south wind; for the
weather, with which no covenant is possible,
shackled you, and the wet waves washed away your
lovely youth.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Like 100D., perhaps elegiac lament rather than epitaph.
<end fn>

<TY>103D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon<fn 1>

  Calliteles was the first to put your figure<fn 2> here;
this one was set up by his descendants<fn 3>: show
gratitude to them.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Ascription doubtful: see C. A. Trypanis, C.Q. 45 (1951) 33.
<fn 2>The lines were inscribed on an Athenian herm, perhaps
c. 450 B.C. (Pfohl), too late for Anacreon.
<fn 3>Or `his grandchildren'.
<end fn>

<TY>104D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon<fn 1>: a dedication to Zeus by Pheidolas

  This horse of Pheidolas from spacious Corinth is
dedicated to the son of Cronus to commemorate the
excellence of his feet.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Note Doric forms, appropriate for a Corinthian patron.
<fn 2>Pheidolas' sons won the horse-race at Olympia in 508 B.C.: see
Paus. 6.13.9 and Page, F.G.E. 401f.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>155

<TY><beg eng>105D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon<fn 1>

  Give Tellias a life to be desired, son of Maia,<fn 2> in
gratitude for these lovely gifts; and grant that he
dwell among the fair-judging people of Euonymon<fn 3>
enjoying good fortune all his days.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Probably a 4th c. poem: a Tellias of Euonymon is known
from an inscription dated c. 325.
<fn 2>Hermes: Tellias may have offered his gifts at a herm.
<fn 3>A deme of Attica.
<end fn>

<TY>106D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon<fn 1>: a dedication to Hermes by Timonax

  Pray that the herald of the gods<fn 2> be kind to
Timonax, who set me up to adorn the lovely porch
and glorify the lord Hermes; I welcome all comers to
the gymnasium, citizen or stranger.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Probably of late date (Trypanis loc. cit.).
<fn 2>Hermes: the herm addresses the passer-by.
<end fn>

<TY>107D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon<fn 1>: a dedication to Dionysus

  In gratitude to you, Dionysus, and as a splendid
adornment to the city<fn 2> I was set up by Echecratidas,
lord of Thessaly.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Note Doric forms. Echecratidas lived in the first half of
the 5th c.
<fn 2>Pharsalus or Athens?<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>157

<TY><beg eng>108D. Palatine Anthology

  This robe was made by Praxidice and designed by

<beg fn>

<TY>109D. Palatine Anthology

<beg fn>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Rather, a description of a painting.

<TY>114D. Palatine Anthology

with your cattle.

<beg fn>

inspired by his bronze Cow see Gow-Page, H. E. ii 63 f.; the
present piece and the next are Hellenistic or later.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>161

<TY><beg eng>115D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon<fn 1>

  This heifer, which was never struck in the mould

Myron pretended to be the work of his own hand.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See 114D. n.1.
<end fn>

<TY>Simonides 101D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon?<fn 1>:

  Leocrates, son of Stroebus, when you dedicated
this statue to Hermes you did not escape the notice
of the fair-tressed Graces.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>There is a lacuna in the ms. before these lines, so that it is
uncertain whether the words `By the same author' refer to
Anacreon; the lines occur also after 6. 213, where they are
ascribed to Simonides. They have been found in an
inscription dated 500-450 B.C. Leocrates was an Athenian
general in 479 and in 459.
<fn 2>The Anthology in both
places has a second couplet, which was not on the stone:
`nor of the delightful Academy, in a corner of which I
proclaim to all visitors your kind service.'
<end fn>

<TY>Simonides 156D. Palatine Anthology

  Anacreon?<fn 1>: a dedication by the tragic poet Sophocles

  Sophocles was the first to set up these altars to the
gods: it was he who won the greatest glory from the
Muse of Tragedy.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>This poem is ascribed to `the same author' as the previous
one; it is clearly later than Anacreon.<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>163

<TT><beg eng>THE ANACREONTEA<fn 1>

<TY>1

  Anacreon, the singer from Teos, saw me and spoke
to me in a dream; and I ran to him and kissed him
and embraced him. He was an old man but handsome,
handsome and amorous; his lips smelled of
wine, and since he was now shaky Love was leading
him by the hand. He took the garland from his head
and gave it to me, and it smelled of Anacreon. Fool
that I was, I held it up and fastened it on my brow -- and
to this very day I have not ceased to be in love.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The title in the ms. is `Convivial poems of Anacreon of
Teos in hemiambics, anacreontea and trimeters':
`anacreontea' is used for `anaclasts' (Ionic dimeters with
anaclasis) as in P. Oxy. 220 col. vii (p. 404 Consbruch) and
later writers (see pp. 285, 316f., 343f. Consbruch); by
`trimeters' may be meant the `cucullii', pairs of ionic
trimeters used by Byzantine writers to separate
anacreontic strophes, but there are none in the ms. (see
West's Preface, p. v).<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>165

<TY>2

  <beg eng>Give<fn 1> me Homer's lyre, but without the murderous
string<fn 2>; bring me cups of ordinances, bring them
after mixing in laws, so that when I am drunk I may
dance and in a sane madness<fn 3> sing to the lyres and
shout the drinking-song.

  Give me Homer's lyre, but without the murderous
string.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Called `a royal song' by the lemmatist: prob. a song for the
symposiarch, who superintended the mixing of wine and
water at a party (cf. LSJ s.v.<end eng> basileu/s <beg eng>V).
<fn 2>Cf. Anacr. eleg. 2.
<fn 3>Cf. Anacr. 356.
<end fn>

<TY>3

  Come, finest of painters, listen to the lyric Muse:
paint first the cities, happy and laughing, and the
playful Bacchants (with their double pipes?)<fn 1>; and if
the wax<fn 2> is able, paint too the customs of lovers.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Lines 3-4 are here placed after 5-6, but the corruption
may be deeper.
<fn 2>On which encaustic painting was done.
<end fn>

<TY>4

  Work your silver, Hephaestus, and make me not a
suit of armour<fn 1> -- what have I to do with battles? -- but

<beg fn>
<fn 1>As for Achilles, Il. 18.<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>167

<beg eng>rather a hollow cup, as deep as you can. Put no
stars on it for me, no Wain,<fn 2> no gloomy Orion: what
do I care about the Pleiads or the fair Ploughman?
Put vines on for me with bunches of grapes on them
and Bacchants picking them; put a wine-press and
men treading it, the satyrs laughing, Loves all in
gold, Cythere<fn 3> laughing together with handsome
Lyaeus,<fn 4> Love and Aphrodite.<fn 5>

<beg fn>
<fn 2>Cf. Il. 18. 485ff.
<fn 3>Aphrodite.
<fn 4>Dionysus, `the
Loosener'.
<fn 5>The version in the Palatine Anthology,
probably the oldest, ends `... no gloomy Orion, but
sprouting vines and laughing bunches of grapes, together
with handsome Lyaeus'; Gellius' version ends `... with
bunches of grapes on them and, all in gold, treading them
together with handsome Lyaeus, Love and Bathyllus.' The
version in the Anacreontea is a clumsy, late expansion:
`Aphrodite' repeats `Cythere', `Love' follows `Loves', 16, 17,
19 have an unexpected choriambic opening (or false
quantities), 18 has a false quantity,<end eng> lhnoba/ths, <beg eng>`treader' (16),
is a late word.
<end fn>

<TY>5

  Fine craftsman, make a springtime cup at once:
the Seasons are bringing us the first delightful roses;
beat the silver thin and make my drink delightful.<end eng>
<PN>169

<beg eng>As for festive rites, I request that you engrave no
loathsome foreign tale: rather put there for us the
child of Zeus, Bacchus, Evius.<fn 1> To initiate us in the
drinking let there be the Cyprian,<fn 2> clapping the
rhythm of the wedding-songs; carve Loves unarmed
and laughing Graces; under a spreading leafy vine
covered with bunches of grapes add handsome
youths, unless Phoebus is playing there.<fn 3>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cult titles of Dionysus.
<fn 2>Aphrodite.
<fn 3>Prosody crude: a late poem.
<end fn>

<TY>6<fn 1>

  Once when I was weaving a garland I found Love
among the roses. I held him by his wings and
plunged him in my wine, then I took it and drank
him down<fn 2>; and now inside my body he tickles me
with his wings.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Ascribed in Planudean Anthology to Julianus; in the
Aldine edition he is identified with a 6th c. prefect of Egypt,
but there is no indication of late date in the poem.
<fn 2>Cf. Anacr. 450.
<end fn>

<TY>7

  The ladies say, `Anacreon, you are old. Take a<end eng>
<PN>171

<beg eng>mirror and look: your hair is no longer there, and
your brow is bare.' But I do not know whether my
hair is still there or has gone; I do know that the
closer Fate is, the more fitting it is for the old man to
enjoy his fun and games.

<TY>8

  I do not care about the wealth of Gyges, lord of
Sardis: I have never envied him, and I have no
grudge against tyrants.<fn 1> I care about drenching my
beard with perfumes, I care about garlanding my
head with roses; I care about today: who knows
tomorrow<fn 2>? So while skies are still cloudless drink,
play dice and pour libation to Lyaeus,<fn 3> lest some
disease come and say, `You must not drink.'

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Vv. 1-4 are based on Archil. 19. 1-3.
<fn 2>The version in the Palatine Anthology ends here.
<fn 3>See 4 n.4.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>173

<TY>9

  <beg eng>Allow me, in heaven's name, to drink, to drink
without stopping for breath: I want to be mad, I want
to be mad. Alcmaeon and white-footed Orestes went
mad when they had killed their mothers<fn 1>: I have
killed no-one, but after drinking the red wine I want
to be mad, I want to be mad. Heracles went mad
once, brandishing his terrible quiver and the bow of
Iphitus<fn 2>; Ajax went mad once, waving Hector's
sword with his shield<fn 3>: I have my cup and this
garland on my hair, no bow and no sword, and I want
to be mad, I want to be mad.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Alcmaeon, son of Amphiaraus and Eriphyle, killed his
traitorous mother on his father's instructions; Orestes
killed Clytemnestra to avenge his father Agamemnon;
`white-footed' perhaps because barefoot in his madness.
<fn 2>Heracles killed Iphitus and took his bow, later using it to
kill his wife Megara and their children.
<fn 3>Ajax's shield was famous (Il. 7. 219); after fighting against him Hector
gave him his sword (Il. 7. 303). Ajax went mad when the
armour of Achilles was awarded to Odysseus.
<end fn>

<TY>10

  What shall I do with you, what shall I do with you,
chattering swallow? Shall I take your nimble wings
and clip them with my scissors or cut out your
tongue like Tereus<fn 1>? Why did you snatch away

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Tereus cut off the tongue of his sister-in-law Philomela so
that she could not tell that he had raped her; she was later

<end fn>
<PN>175

<beg eng>Bathyllus from my lovely dreams with your early

<TY>11

  A youth was selling a wax Love. I stopped by his
side and said, `How much do you want for your
handiwork?' He replied in Doric, `Take him at your
price. To tell you the whole story, I am no waxmodeller:
it's just that I have no wish to live with
Love, the villain.' `Then give me him, give me him for
a drachma: he'll make a fine bedfellow.' Love, set me
on fire at once: if you don't, you will melt in flames.

<TY>12

  Some say half-woman Attis went mad shouting for
lovely Cybebe in the mountains.<fn 1> Some drink the

<beg fn>

<beg eng>babbling water of bay-bearing Phoebus by the slopes

<beg fn>

<TY>13

  I want to love, I want to love.<fn 1> Love urged me to
love, but I was a fool and was not persuaded. So he
immediately took up his bow and golden quiver and
challenged me to a fight. I hung my corslet from my
shoulders, like Achilles, and took my spears and oxhide
shield and began fighting with Love. He shot
and I ran; when he had no arrows left, he was
distressed; then he hurled himself for a javelin,
pierced the middle of my heart and loosened my
limbs. My shield (and spears and corslet)<fn 2> are useless:
why hurl weapons from me when the fight is
within me?

<beg fn>
<fn 1>West deletes the line.
<fn 2>Crusius' supplement.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>179

<TY>14

  <beg eng>If you can count all the leaves of the trees or find
the total of the waves in the whole sea, then I
appoint you sole computer of my loves. First enter
twenty loves from Athens, plus fifteen; next, whole
series of loves from Corinth: it is in Achaea, where
women are beautiful.<fn 1> Enter my loves from Lesbos
and all the way to Ionia, Caria and Rhodes, two
thousand. What's that? You're dazed<fn 2>? I haven't yet
mentioned Syria or the passions of Canobus<fn 3> or of
Crete, which has everything, where Love holds his
revels in the cities. Why should I number my heart's
loves beyond Cadiz or those in Bactria and India?

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Corinth was in the Achaean Confederacy from 243 B.C.
till its destruction in 146 B.C.; refounded in 44 B.C., it
became capital of the province of Achaea; cf. Iliad 3. 75,258
`Achaea of the beautiful women'.
<fn 2>Text and translation
doubtful.
<fn 3>Or Canopus, town in Egypt near Alexandria.<end eng>

<TY>15

  <beg eng>Lovely pigeon, where, where have you flown from?
As you race on the air you smell of perfumes, you
rain perfumes: where did they all come from? Who
are you and what is your business?

  `Anacreon sent me to a boy, to Bathyllus, now lord
and master of all. Cythere<fn 1> sold me in return for a
little song; and it is for Anacreon that I perform
tasks like this. And what letters I am carrying from
him now! And he says he will give me my freedom
immediately; but if he does release me, I shall stay
with him as his slave. Why should I fly over mountains
and fields and sit on trees after eating some
wild food? As things are, I eat bread which I snatch
from Anacreon's own hands, and for drink he gives
me the wine which he drinks to his loves; after
drinking I begin to dance, and while my master plays
his lyre I shade him with my wings. When he goes to

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See 4 n.3.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>183

<beg eng>bed, I sleep on the lyre itself. There, you know it all.

<TY>16

  Come, best of painters! Paint, best of painters,
master of the Rhodian art<fn 1>! Paint my absent girl
according to my instructions. First paint her soft
black hair; and if the wax<fn 2> is able, make it smell of
perfume. Paint her whole cheek and then her ivory
brow beneath her dark hair. Do not part her
eyebrows nor run them together, but let her keep, as
in real life, the black rims of her eyes meeting
imperceptibly. Now make her eyes as they are, from
fire, both flashing, like Athena's, and moist, like
Cythere's.<fn 3> Paint her nose and her cheeks, mingling

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Painting.
<fn 2>See 3 n.2.
<fn 3>See 4 n.3.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>185

<beg eng>roses and cream. Paint her lips like Persuasion's,
provoking kisses. Under her soft chin let all the
Graces fly around her marble-white neck. Dress the
rest of her in robes of light purple, but let her skin
show through a little to prove the quality of her
body. Enough -- I can see her! Soon, wax, you will be
talking too.

<TY>17

  Paint my beloved Bathyllus according to my prescription:
make his hair shine, dark beneath but
with the ends lightened by the sun; add curling locks
falling freely in disorder and let them lie where they
wish. Let his soft dewy forehead be garlanded with
eyebrows darker than snakes. Let his black eyes be a
mixture of ferocity and serenity, taking their ferocity
from Ares, their serenity from beautiful Cythere,<fn 1>
so that he may inspire terror and also hopeful
suspense. Make his downy cheek as rosy as an apple,

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See 4 n.3.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>187

<beg eng>and, if possible, add a blush like that of Modesty. I do
not yet know how you are to make his lip soft and
full of persuasion: but let the wax<fn 2> itself have
everything, talking silently. After his face make an
ivory neck finer than that of Adonis. Give him the
chest and two hands of Hermes, the thighs of Polydeuces,
the belly of Dionysus<fn 3>; above his soft thighs,
thighs with raging fire in them, put a simple member
that already desires the Paphian.<fn 4> But your art is
grudging: you cannot show his back; that would
have been better. Why should I describe the feet?
Take your fee, as much as you ask. Take<fn 5> down this
Apollo and create Bathyllus; and if ever you come to
Samos, paint Phoebus from Bathyllus.

<beg fn>
<fn 2>See 3 n.2.
<fn 3>Adonis the type of youthful beauty;
Hermes the ideal young athlete of the sculptors;
Polydeuces (or Pollux) the boxer; Dionysus the youthful
god of later Greek art.
<fn 4>Aphrodite, who rose from the
sea near Paphos in Cyprus.
<fn 5>The last four lines
introduce metrical variations and may be a later addition:
`this Apollo' is presumably a picture which is to be replaced
by the painting of Bathyllus.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>189

<TY>18

  <beg eng>Ladies, give me, give me some Bromian<fn 1> to drink
without stopping for breath, for I am already betrayed<fn 2>
by the heat and am groaning. Give me
garlands of his flowers; give me them so that I may
wreath my burned brow. But, my heart, with what
shall I ward off the heat of the Loves? I<fn 3> shall sit in
the shade of Bathyllus: that is a fine tree, and it
shakes its soft tresses on the tenderest of branches;
and nearby (whispers?) a spring that flows with
persuasion. Who could see such a resting-place and
pass it by?

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Wine: the Bromian is Dionysus.
<fn 2>Perhaps `fevered'.
<fn 3>The scribe of our ms. corrected himself to indicate the

<TY>24

  Nature gave bulls horns, horses hooves, hares
speed, lions a wide mouth full of teeth, fish power to
swim, birds flight, men wisdom, women -- she had
nothing left. And so? She gives them beauty, strong
as any shield, strong as any sword. A beautiful
woman overcomes even steel or fire.

<TY>25

  Dear swallow, you come every year and weave
your nest in summer, but in winter you disappear, off
to the Nile or Memphis; whereas Love is always
weaving his nest in my heart: one Desire is getting
his wings, another is still an egg, another is halfhatched<end eng>
<PN>197

<beg eng>already; and there is a continuous shouting
from the wide-mouthed chicks; little baby Loves are
fed by bigger ones, and when they are fully grown
they immediately beget others in their turn. What
remedy can there be? I have not the strength to
shout down<fn 1> all these Loves.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Perhaps `to chase them out by shouting'.
<end fn>

<TY>26

  You tell the story of Thebes, another tells of the
war-cries of the Phrygians,<fn 1> I tell how I myself was
captured. It was no horse<fn 2> that destroyed me, no
infantry, no fleet, but another strange kind of army,
striking me with its eyes.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. the Trojans.
<fn 2>As at Troy; or perhaps `cavalry'.
<end fn>

<TY>27

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. of the branding iron.<end eng>

<TY>28

  <beg eng>Cythere's husband<fn 1> was making the Loves'

and Love was adding gall. One day Ares came in
from the battlefield brandishing his strong spear and
began to make fun of Love's weapon. Love said, `This
one is heavy: try it and you will see.' Ares took the
javelin,<fn 3> while the Cyprian smiled quietly; and with
a groan he said, `It is heavy: take it back.' `Keep it,'
said Love.<fn 4>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Hephaestus: Cythere = Aphrodite.
<fn 2>Aphrodite.

<TY>29

  It is hard not to fall in love, it is hard to fall in

the man who first loved money! Thanks to it we lose
brothers and parents; thanks to it there are wars and

<beg fn>

<end fn>
<PN>201

<beg eng>murders; and, worst of all, thanks to it we lovers are

<TY>30

  In a dream I seemed to be running, with wings on
my shoulders, and Love, with shoes of lead on his
pretty feet, was pursuing me and catching me up.
What is the meaning of this dream? I think it means
that though I have been entangled in many loves and
have wriggled free from all the others, I am caught
fast in this one.

<TY>31

  Love, beating me cruelly with a rod tied round
with hyacinths,<fn 1> ordered me to run by his side; and
as I ran through fierce torrents and thickets and
gullies the sweat distressed me, my heart climbed to
my nose and I might have perished; but Love fanned<fn 2>
my brow with his tender wings and said, `Can't
you love, then?'

<beg fn>

<TY>32

  <beg eng>I want to make a couch of soft myrtles and lotus
plants and drink to my friends; let Love tie his tunic
at his neck with papyrus cord and serve me with
wine: for life rolls swiftly on like a chariot-wheel,
and we shall lie, a handful of dust, when our bones
have been loosened.<fn 1> Why perfume a stone? Why
pour wine uselessly for soil? No, perfume me while I
am still alive, garland my head with roses, summon
my girl: before I depart, Love, to join the dances of
the dead, I want to scatter my cares.<fn 2>

<beg fn>

<fn 2>Strongly influenced by Theognis, e.g. 568, 878,

<TY>33

  Once in the middle of the night, at the hour when
the Bear<fn 1> is already turning by the Ploughman's
hand<fn 2> and all the tribes of mortals lie overcome by
exhaustion, Love stood at my bolted door and began
knocking. `Who's banging my door?' I said: `You've

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The Great Bear: the Wain, Plough or Dipper.
<fn 2>In the constellation Bootes: cf. Il. 18. 487 ff., Od. 5. 272 ff.<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>205

<beg eng>shattered my dreams.' Love said, `Open up! I'm a
baby: don't be afraid. I am getting wet, and I have
been wandering about in the moonless night.' When
I heard this I felt sorry for him and immediately lit a
lamp and opened the door and saw a baby with bow,
wings and quiver. I made him sit by the hearth,
warmed his hands in my palms and squeezed the
water from his hair. When the cold had relaxed its
grip, he said, `Come, let's try this bow to see if the
string has been at all damaged by the rain.' He drew
it and hit me right in the heart,<fn 3> like a stinging gadfly;
and he leaped up chuckling and said, `Stranger,
rejoice with me: my bow is undamaged; but your
heart will be sore.'

<beg fn>
<fn 3>Lit. `in the middle of the liver'.
<end fn>

<TY>34

  We count you blessed, cicada, when on the treetops,
having drunk a little dew, you sing like a king:
you own everything that you see in the fields, everything<end eng>
<PN>207

<beg eng>that the woods produce. You (spare?) farmers,
robbing none of them. You are honoured by mortals
as the sweet prophet of summer. The Muses love you
and Phoebus himself loves you and has given you a
clear song. Age does not distress you, wise one,
earth-born,<fn 1> song-lover! You who do not suffer, you
whose flesh is bloodless, you are almost like the
gods.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The Athenians in the early days wore gold cicada-clasps
in their hair, probably to mark their claim to be
autochthonous (see Thuc. 1.2.5, 1.6.3). Ar. Hist. Anim.
5.30 (556b) notes that the cicada larva develops in the
ground, so that the insect is literally `earth-born'.
<end fn>

<TY>35

  Love once failed to notice a bee that was sleeping
among the roses, and he was wounded: he was struck

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Aphrodite.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>209

<TY>36

  If Wealth offered life to mortals for gold, then I
would persevere in hoarding it, so that if Death came
he could take some and pass on. But since mortals
cannot buy life, why should I groan in vain, why
weep and wail? If I am fated to die, what use is gold?
Let me drink, then, and when I have drunk the sweet
wine join my friends or on a soft bed perform
Aphrodite's rites.

<TY>37

  While I was sleeping at night under sea-purple

<beg fn>

<beg eng>words on account of the pretty girls. But when I

<TY>38

  Let us be merry and drink wine and sing of Bacchus,
the inventor of the choral dance, the lover of
all songs, leading the same life as the Loves, the
darling of Cythere<fn 1>; thanks to him Drunkenness
was brought forth, the Grace was born, Pain takes a
rest and Trouble goes to sleep. So the drink is mixed
and tender boys are bringing it, and grief has fled,
mingling with the wind-fed storm: let us take our
drink, then, and let our worries go: what is the good
of hurting yourself with cares? How can we know
the future? Man's life is unclear. I want to be drunk
and dance, to perfume myself and have fun (with the
handsome youths and)<fn 2> with beautiful women too.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Aphrodite.
<fn 2>A line to this effect seems to have been
lost.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>213

<beg eng>Those who wish can bother with worries.

  Let us be merry and drink wine and sing of
Bacchus.

<TY>39

  I love a pleasant old man, I love a young dancer:
and if the old man dances, then he is old as far as his
hair is concerned, but young at heart.

<TY>40

  Since<fn 1> I was created a mortal to journey on the
path of life, I can tell the years that I have gone past,
but do not know the years I have to run. Let me go,
worries: let there be no dealings between you and
me. Before death catches up with me, I shall play, I
shall laugh and I shall dance with lovely Lyaeus.<fn 2>

<beg fn>

<fn 2>Dionysus.<end eng>

<TY>41

<TY>42

<TY>43

  Let us fasten garlands of roses on our brows and

<TY>44

  <beg eng>Let us mix the Loves' rose with Dionysus<fn 1>: let us
fasten on our brows the rose with its lovely petals
and drink, laughing gently. Rose, finest of flowers,

and in your precinct, Dionysus, I shall play the lyre

<TY>55

  Along with spring, the bringer of garlands, I am
eager to sing with clear voice of spring's companion,<fn 1>
the soft rose. It is the breath of the gods and
the joy of mortals, the glory of the Graces in springtime,
the delight of the Loves with their rich garlands
and of Aphrodite; it is a subject for poetry and
the graceful plant of the Muses; it is sweet to find
when one is picking one's way along thorny paths,
sweet to take and warm in soft hands, to press to
one's body, the light flower of Love.<fn 1> At feasts,
banquets and festivals of Dionysus what should we

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Text and translation uncertain.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>233

<beg eng>do without the rose? Rosy-fingered Dawn, rosy-armed
Nymphs, rosy-hued Aphrodite -- so the poets

defies time: for the rose in its graceful old age keeps
the fragrance of its youth. Come, let us tell of its
birth: when from the grey waters the sea gave birth

a fearful sight for Olympus, then earth made
wonderful new shoots of roses blossom, her creation
of skilled artistry; and that the rose might resemble
the blessed gods, Lyaeus<fn 4> sprinkled it with nectar
and made it flourish proudly on the thorn, an immortal
plant.

<TY>56

the grape-bunches they may all stay healthy -- healthy

mind -- till the next year appears.

<TY>58

  When Gold, the runaway, flees from me on nimble
wind-swift feet -- and he is always fleeing, always -- I
do not pursue him: who wants to chase what he
hates? As soon as I am parted from Gold, the runaway,<end eng>
<PN>239

<beg eng>I give my mind's cares to the winds to carry
off, and I take my lyre and sing love-songs. But just
when my heart teaches me to despise him, suddenly
the runaway speaks to me again, bringing me drunken
ideas to make me take him and neglect my sweet
lyre. Faithless, faithless Gold! In vain do you cast a
spell on me with your tricks: the lyre-strings, more
than gold, hold sweet desires. You give men a love of
trickeries and jealousies, but the lyre mixes cups of
desires that bring no harm to bridal chambers and
chaste kisses. When you want to, you run away; but I
would not leave my lyre's song for a moment. You
give pleasure to tricky, faithless strangers instead of
the Muses; but I, the lyre-player, have the Muse
making her home in my heart. You may raise your
lament, you may polish up your glitter<fn 1>!

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Text and translation of last sentence insecure.<end eng>

<TY>53

<TY>60(a)

  <beg eng>I shall make the lyre-strings vibrate. This is no
prize-competition: but everyone who has attained
the finest skill in poetry must practise his art. Striking
a clear melody with my ivory plectrum I shall
shout in Phrygian rhythm<fn 1> like a swan of the Cayster<fn 2>
singing with its wings a complex song in
unison with the wind's cry. Muse, dance with me: for
the lyre like the bay<fn 3> and the tripod is sacred to
Phoebus. My theme is the love of Phoebus, that
unfulfilled frenzy -- for the girl remains chaste: she
escaped the sting of his passion and changed her
bodily form to take root as a flourishing plant; and
Phoebus came, Phoebus, believing that he was
master of the girl; but he plucked the green leaves,
thinking that he was performing the rites of
Cythere.<fn 4>

<fn 4>Aphrodite. There may be

<TY>60B. Hippolytus, Refutation of all the Heresies

  This, he says, is the cup<end eng> [1ko/ndu]1 <beg eng>from which the king
drinks and which he uses for divination. This is the cup
that was found hidden among the fine grain of Benjamin.<fn 1>
And the Greeks, he says, mention it also in these wild
words:

  Bring water, bring wine, boy: make me drunk and
stupefy me<fn 2>: my cup tells me what must become of
me.

This, he says, would be sufficient for men if only it were
understood, this cup of Anacreon which without speaking
tells of a secret mystery: Anacreon's cup, he says, is
unspeaking, the cup which Anacreon says tells him with
unspeaking voice what must become of him ...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See Genesis 44. 1-5.
<fn 2>Cf. Anacreont. 52(b). 3.<end eng>

<TY>1 Suda, Olympus<fn 1>

  A Phrygian, the younger Olympus of the two, a
piper; lived<fn 2> in the time of Midas<fn 3> son of Gordius.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Suda (O 219) lists also `Olympus, a Maeonian from Mysia,
a piper and a composer of songs and elegiacs, the earliest
performer of instrumental music for the pipes; pupil and
favourite of Marsyas, who was a Satyr by birth and was the
pupil and son of Hyagnis. Olympus lived before the Trojan
Wars. The mountain in Mysia is named after him'; also
(O 220) `Olympus, who devised and taught the tunes of
cithara-song'. The earlier Olympus, pupil of the Satyr
Marsyas and wrestler with Pan (Plin. N.H. 36. 5. 35), is
presumably fictitious; the view that there were two
musicians of the name appears first in Pratinas (713(i)
P.M.G.) and Glaucus of Rhegium.
<fn 2>Less probably,
`born'.
<fn 3>King of Phrygia (738-696 B.C.): cf. Terp. test. 5.

<TY>3 `Plutarch', On Music

  ...I shall now pass to music for the pipes. The
above-mentioned Olympus, one of the pipers from
Phrygia, is said to have composed a pipe tune for
Apollo, the so-called Many-headed nome. This
Olympus, they say, was one of the descendants of the
first Olympus,<fn 1> Marsyas' pupil, who had composed
his nomes in honour of the gods: this Olympus, who
was the favourite of Marsyas and learned his piping
from him, introduced to Greece the enharmonic<fn 2>
nomes which the Greeks now use in their festivals
for the gods. Others say that the Many-headed nome
is the work of Crates,<fn 3> a pupil of Olympus, but
Pratinas<fn 4> says it is by the younger Olympus.

  The so-called Chariot nome is said to have been
composed by the first Olympus, pupil of Marsyas.
... That it is by him can be gathered from Glaucus<fn 5>
On the Ancient Poets, where one can learn also that
Stesichorus of Himera imitated not Orpheus nor
Terpander nor Archilochus nor Thaletas but
Olympus, using the Chariot nome and the dactylic

<TY>5 `Plutarch', On Music

  Olympus, as Aristoxenus<fn 1> says, is supposed by
musical authorities to have been the discoverer of
the enharmonic genus,<fn 2> everything before him having
been diatonic or chromatic. They suspect that
his discovery happened as follows: Olympus was
working in the diatonic and often making his melody
move to the diatonic parhypate (F), sometimes from
the paramese (b), sometimes from the mese (a), bypassing
the diatonic lichanos (G); and so he realised
the beauty of the melody's character, and came in
this way to admire and adopt the system founded on
its analogy, composing with these intervals in the
Dorian mode ....

  These then were the first enharmonic compositions;
later the semitone (F-E) was divided in both
the Lydian and the Phrygian modes. Clearly

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Musical theorist, 4th c. B.C.
<fn 2>I.e. the tetrachord
a F E* E, where E* denotes the quarter-tone above E;
the diatonic is a G F E.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>279

<beg eng>Olympus advanced music by introducing something
that was original and unknown to his predecessors,
and was the founder of Greek, i.e. of beautiful,
music.

<TY>6 `Plutarch', On Music

  For Aristoxenus On Music Book 1 says that
Olympus was the first to play on his pipes a lament
for the Python<fn 1> in the Lydian mode.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The serpent of Delphi, killed by Apollo.
<end fn>

<TY>7 `Plutarch', On Music

  Similarly it was not because they did not know all
the modes that they used only some of them: it was
not ignorance that caused their narrow range and
their use of few notes or that made Olympus and
Terpander and those who followed their chosen
practice avoid the use of many notes and complexity.
The compositions of Olympus and Terpander and all
kindred spirits are the proof: they use three notes<fn 1>
and are simple, but they are superior to compositions
that are complex and use many notes, so that
no one can copy Olympus' style, and those who use
many notes and a variety of scales fall short of him.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>a F E: see test. 5.
<end fn>

<TY>8 `Plutarch', On Music

  Again, the famous Olympus, who is credited with
the foundation of Greek and nomic music, is said to<end eng>
<PN>281

<beg eng>have invented the enharmonic genus<fn 1> and among
rhythms the prosodiac,<fn 2> in which the nome of Ares is
composed, and the choree,<fn 3> of which he made much
use in his compositions for the Great Mother. Some
think Olympus also invented the bacchius.<fn 4>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See test. 5.
<fn 2>Term applied to various dactylic lengths,
e.g. <*>|-<*>|-<*>|
<fn 3>Term used of trochee (-<*>) or
tribrach (<*>)
<fn 4><*>--, another rhythm with `five in the
bar': see test. 4.
<end fn>

<TY>9 `Plutarch', On Music

  This (sc. the moral character of a piece of music),
we say, is brought about by a combination or a blend
of elements or by both. Take for example Olympus'
setting of the enharmonic genus in the Phrygian
mode and his blending of it with the paeon epibatos<fn 1>:
this is what created the moral character of
the opening of the nome of Athena. For when you
add the handling of the melody and rhythm and
skilfully change the rhythm by itself so that it
becomes trochaic instead of paeonic, then you have
the enharmonic genus of Olympus. Moreover, if you
keep the enharmonic genus and the Phrygian mode
and the whole tetrachord system as well, the moral
character can be much altered: for the so-called
`harmony' in the nome of Athena is very different in
character from the introduction.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>A sequence of 5 long syllables.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>283

and Lydian, that of Sacadas was Pythian, those
of Euius cyclic, those of Olympus for performance at
tombs.<fn 1>

<TY><beg eng>13 Plato, Minos

  Socrates: Who in the days of old was a good
lawgiver in the laws<fn 1> of pipe-playing? ... Is it not
Marsyas who is meant, and his favourite, Olympus
the Phrygian?

  Companion: That is correct.

  Socrates: And their pipe music is most divine, and
it alone stirs up and reveals those who are in need of
the gods; and to this day it alone remains, since it is
divine.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Socrates puns on<end eng> no/mos, <beg eng>`law' or `tune'.
<fn 2>Cf. Pl. Ion 533b, Luc. Indoct. 5, Ael. V.H. 13.20.
<end fn>

<TY>14 Aristotle, Politics

  This would be clear if our characters could be
shown to be affected by music: but many instances
do make this plain, and not least among them the
tunes of Olympus; for they, it is generally agreed,
make our souls excited, and excitement is a condition
of the character of the soul.<end eng>
