<TY>2 Eusebius, Chronicle<fn 1>

  (a) Olympiad 5.1 (760/759 B.C.): Eumelus the poet,
who composed the Bugonia and Tales of Europa, and
Arctinus, who composed the Aethiopis, and The Sack
of Troy are all well-known.

  (b) Olympiad 9.1 (744/743 B.C.): Eumelus the
Corinthian poet is well-known; also the Sibyl of
Erythrae.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See A. A. Mosshammer, The Chronicle of Eusebius
198-203.<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>289

<TY><beg eng>3 Pausanias, Description of Greece

  Eumelus, son of Amphilytus, one of the so-called
Bacchids,<fn 1> who is said to have composed the epic
poems, states in his History of Corinth, if indeed it is
his work,<fn 2> that ...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. the Bacchiads, the clan who ruled Corinth from c. 750
to c. 657 B.C.
<fn 2>Cf. Paus. 4.4. 1 below.<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>291

<TT><beg eng>EUMELUS

<WW>PROCESSIONAL HYMN TO DELOS<fn 1>

<TY>1 (a) Pausanias, Description of Greece (on Messenia)

  They keep an annual festival, the Ithomaea, and in
ancient times they used also to hold a contest in music.
This can be proved by the lines of Eumelus among other
things: at any rate he wrote the following in his
Processional Hymn to Delos:

<TX begin>For the god of Ithome<fn 2> took pleasure in the Muse,
the pure Muse<fn 3> wearing her free sandals.<fn 4><TX end>

I think he composed these lines because he knew that the
Messenians held a music contest.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See C. M. Bowra, C.Q. 57 (1963) 145 ff.
<fn 2>Zeus.
<fn 3>Or, supplementing the text, `the Muse with her pure
lyre'.
<fn 4>The lines seem to indicate that Messenia's
freedom is already threatened. The first quarrel with
Sparta took place in the reign of Phintas (Paus. 4.4.1: see
next note).
<end fn>

(b) Pausanias, Description of Greece (on Messenia)

  In the time of Phintas,<fn 1> son of Sybotas, the Messenians
first sent a sacrifice and a chorus of men to Apollo at Delos.
Their processional song to the god was taught them by
Eumelus, and these lines are reckoned to be his only
genuine work.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Father of Androcles and Antiochus, who were kings of
Messenia at the beginning of the first war (c. 740-720 B.C.).
Eumelus may have named Phintas in his poem.<end eng>
<end fn>

<PN>293

<beg eng>(c) Pausanias, Description of Greece (on Olympia)

  I was quite unable to gather who had created the chest,<fn 1>
but the inscriptions on it could have been composed by
someone else, and my inclination was to call them the work
of Eumelus of Corinth, mainly on the strength of his
Processional Hymn to Delos.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The `Chest of Cypselus' in the temple of Hera.
<fn 2>But the chest is dated to the late 7th or early 6th c.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>295

<TT><beg eng>TERPANDER

<WW>LIFE AND WORKS

<WW>BIOGRAPHY

<TY>1 Suda, Terpander

  From Arne,<fn 1> or from Antissa in Lesbos,<fn 2> or from
Cyme.<fn 3> Some have made him a descendant of Hesiod,
others of Homer, calling him son of Boeus of Phocis,
son of Euryphon, son of Homer. A lyric poet, who
first gave the lyre seven strings<fn 4> and was the first to
write lyric nomes,<fn 5> though some attribute this to
Philammon.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Perhaps the Boeotian Arne: one of the cithara-nomes was
called `Boeotian' (test. 19), and Hesiod settled in
Boeotia.
<fn 2>His birthplace is usually given as Antissa
(e.g. by Steph. Byz. s.v.<end eng> A)/ntissa]1 <beg eng>or Lesbos, but Diodorus
8.28 (ap. Tzetz. Chil. 1. 388 ff.) calls him `T. of Methymna'.
<fn 3>Birthplace of Hesiod.
<fn 4>Cf. testt. 14, 16, fr. 6.
<fn 5>Cf. testt. 18-20.
<end fn>

<WW>CHRONOLOGY

<TY>2 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  That Terpander too is earlier than Anacreon is
clear from the following facts: Terpander was the
first victor ever at the Carnea,<fn 1> as Hellanicus<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Spartan festival in honour of Apollo: cf. test. 10 n. 2.
<fn 2>5th c. B.C. historian and mythographer from Lesbos.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>297

<beg eng>records in his Carnean Victors, both the metrical
and the prose versions; and the Carnea were
established in the 26th Olympiad (676/672 B.C.), as
Sosibius says in his work On Chronology. Secondly,
Hieronymus<fn 3> in his treatise On Cithara-singers,
which is Book 5 of his work On Poets, says Terpander
lived in the time of Lycurgus the lawgiver, who
is universally agreed to have organized the first
numbered Olympic Games<fn 4> along with Iphitus of
Elis.<fn 5>

<beg fn>
<fn 3>3rd c. B.C. philosopher and literary historian.
<fn 4>I.e. in 776 B.C., but the date is at least a century too early for
Terpander; for his alleged association with Lycurgus see
also Plut. Agis 10.
<fn 5>For the mistaken view that
Terpander and Hipponax were contemporary see `Plut.'
Mus. 6.
<end fn>

<TY>3 Parian Marble

  From the time when the Lesbian Terpander, son of
Derdenes, introduced the new nomes of cithara-song
and altered the earlier style of music 381 years<fn 1>;
Dropides was archon at Athens.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. 645/644 B.C., 381 years before 264/263.
<end fn>

<TY>4 Eusebius, Chronicle

  Olympiad 34.3 (642/641 B.C.)<fn 1>: the musician Terpander
is regarded as famous.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>A variant reading gives 641/640. For the calculations of
Eusebius see A.A. Mosshammer, The Chronicle of Eusebius
226 ff.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>299

<TY><beg eng>5 Clement of Alexandria, Miscellanies

  Some indeed make Terpander ancient: Hellanicus
at any rate says that he lived in the time of Midas,<fn 1>
but Phanias,<fn 2> putting Lesches of Lesbos earlier than
Terpander, makes Terpander later than Archilochus<fn 3> ...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Or `was born ...'; Midas' dates are 738-696 B.C.: cf.
Olympus test. 1.
<fn 2>Phaenias of Eresus, literary historian (4th c. B.C.).
<fn 3>A.'s dates are c. 680-640 B.C.; see test. 6.
<end fn>

<TY>6 `Plutarch', On Music

  Terpander appears to have excelled in the art of
cithara-singing<fn 1>: it is recorded that he won four
successive victories at the Pythian Games.
Moreover, he belongs to very early times: Glaucus of
Italy<fn 2> in a book On the Ancient Poets and Musicians
makes him older than Archilochus, saying that Terpander
came second after the first composers of song
sung to the pipes.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Themistius Or. 26.316c.
<fn 2>See Olympus test. 3 n. 5.
<end fn>

<WW>LIFE

<TY>7 Aelian, Historical Miscellanies<fn 1>

  The Spartans had no literary skill, being concerned
rather with gymnastics and military training.
If ever they needed help from the Muses in
sickness or madness or any other such civic disaster,

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Heraclides Lembus, Excerpta Politiarum (p. 373 Rose,
p. 16 Dilts).<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>301

<beg eng>they would send for foreigners, doctors or purifiers,
in accordance with a pronouncement from the Delphic
oracle. For example, they sent for Terpander
and Thaletas and Tyrtaeus and Nymphaeus of
Cydonia and Alcman, who was a Lydian.

<TY>8 Clement of Alexandria, Miscellanies

  Terpander of Antissa was the first to supply
melody for his poems, and he set the laws of the
Spartans to music.

<TY>9 Suda<fn 1>: `next to the Lesbian singer'

  A proverbial expression for those who take second
place, since the Lesbian cithara-singers were the
first to be invited by the Spartans: when their city
was in a state of unrest, they were told by the oracle
to send for the Lesbian singer; so sending for Terpander,
who was in exile from Antissa<fn 2> because of a
murder, they listened to him at their public dinners
and were restored to calm. The<fn 3> Spartans in time of
civil strife sent to Lesbos for the musician Terpander,
who brought their souls into harmony and

<beg fn>
<fn 1>So Photius Lexicon i 418. 7 ff. Naber; see also Aelius
Dionysius (ap. Eust. Il. 741.17) = Aristot. Spartan
Constitution fr. 545 Rose, Philodemus Mus. i fr. 30.31 ff.,
iv col. 19. 4 ff., 20. 1 ff. (the Epicurean Philodemus ridicules
the story which had been transmitted by the Stoic
Diogenes of Babylon: see von Arnim Stoic. Vet. Fragm.
iii 232), Diodorus 8.28 (ap. Tzetz. Chil. 1. 389 ff.), `Plut.'
Mus. 42 (=Thaletas test. 4), Zenobius Cent. 5.9, Sappho
106.
<fn 2>Or `sending to Antissa for T., who was in exile ...'.
<fn 3>A second version of the story.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>303

<beg eng>stopped their strife. So after that, whenever the
Spartans listened to some musician, they said, `Next
to the Lesbian singer'.

<TY>10 Palatine Anthology: Tryphon

  Terpes,<fn 1> while singing to the strumming of his
sweet lyre among the Sunshades,<fn 2> died after returning
to his home in Sparta. He was not struck by a
sword nor by a missile: he died when he was struck
on the lips by one single fig. Alas, Death is never at a
loss for an occasion.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Doubtfully equated with Terpander, e.g. by Suda<end eng> G <beg eng>315,
where he is said to have choked when a fig was thrown in
his mouth while he was singing.
<fn 2>Tent-like structures used at the Carnea in Sparta (Athen. 4.141 f).
<end fn>

<WW>MUSIC AND POETRY

<TY>11 `Plutarch', On Music

  Now music was organized for the first time<fn 1> by
Terpander at Sparta.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>For the second `organization' see Thaletas test. 7.
<end fn>

<TY>12 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Posidonius<fn 1> does not realize that the magadis is
an ancient instrument, although Pindar clearly says
that Terpander invented the barbitos in answer to
the Lydian pectis, `the barbitos which once Terpander
the Lesbian was the first to invent when he heard

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Philosopher and historian, 1st c. B.C.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>305

<beg eng>at Lydian feasts the plucked strings of the lofty
pectis sounding in answer to it'<fn 2>; and the pectis and
magadis are the same thing ...

<beg fn>
<fn 2>I.e. in unison, the barbitos being probably an octave
lower; but Pindar may have meant `sounding in answer to
the voice of the singer'.
<end fn>

<TY>13 `Plutarch', On Music

  For those who have investigated these things have
credited Terpander with the invention of the Dorian
nete,<fn 1> since his predecessors did not use it in their
melodies; and he is said to have invented the whole
Mixolydian mode<fn 2> and the style of orthian melody
which uses the orthios foot,<fn 3> and to have invented
also the marked trochee in addition to the orthios.
Also, as Pindar says, Terpander invented the music
of scolia.<fn 4>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The highest note of the scale: cf. test. 14.
<fn 2>Invention ascribed to Sappho in Mus. 16.
<fn 3>-- <*>; for the marked trochee see West, Greek Metre 55 f.
<fn 4>Drinking songs.
<end fn>

<TY>14 Aristotle, Problems

  Why is the diapason<fn 1> so called and not named a
diocto (`eighth') after the number of notes, like the
diatessaron (`fourth') and the diapente (`fifth')? Is it
because there were originally seven strings, and
then Terpander removed trite and added nete, and in
his time it was called diapason and not diocto, since
there were seven notes?

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The octave, literally `the interval of all the notes'.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>307

<TY><beg eng>15 Timotheus, The Persians

  First Orpheus of the intricate music, son of Calliope,
begot the tortoise-shell lyre ... from Pieria.
After him Terpander yoked the Muse to ten songs<fn 1>:
Aeolian Lesbos bore him to give glory to Antissa.
Now Timotheus with measures and rhythms of
eleven strings brings the lyre to life ...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Perhaps `to songs on ten strings', but cf. testt. 1, 14, 16, 17,
fr. 6.
<end fn>

<TY>16 `Plutarch', On Music

  For the lyre had seven notes as far back as the
time of Terpander of Antissa,<fn 1> but Timotheus took
the plunge and increased the number.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. fr. 6, Pliny N.H. 7. 62.204 (T. added three strings to the
original four); Boethius Mus. 1.20 says the number was
suggested by the seven planets. But `Plut.' Mus.
18 = Olympus test. 7 speaks of only three notes.
<end fn>

<TY>17 Plutarch, Spartan Customs

  If anyone tried to break the rules of the old music
in any way, they (sc. the Spartans) would not allow
it. Even Terpander, one of the older musicians, the
best cithara-singer of his day and a praiser of the
deeds of the heroes, was none the less fined by the
ephors, who took his lyre and nailed it to a wall, all
because he fitted one extra string to give variety of
sound: only the simpler type of melody met with
their approval.<end eng>
<PN>309

<TY><beg eng>18 `Plutarch', On Music

  So, according to him (sc. Heraclides<fn 1>), Terpander,
a composer of nomes for cithara-singing, set to music
in each nome his own hexameters<fn 2> and Homer's and
sang them in the contests; and he says that Terpander
was the first to supply names<fn 3> for the nomes of
cithara-song.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Heraclides Ponticus, 4th c. B.C. philosopher.
<fn 2>Cf. Proclus Chrest. 45.
<fn 3>See test. 19.
<end fn>

<TY>19 `Plutarch', On Music

  The nomes sung to the cithara were established in
the time of Terpander, shortly before those for the
pipes: he at any rate gave them their names before
the pipe-nomes got theirs, calling them Boeotian and
Aeolian, Trochaios (`trochaic') and Oxys (`highpitched'),
Cepion<fn 1> and Terpandrean, and also Tetraoidios
(`four-songed').<fn 2> Terpander also composed
hexameter preludes to be sung to the cithara.<fn 3>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Supposedly named after a favourite pupil (Pollux 4. 65,
`Plut.' Mus. 6).
<fn 2>Pollux 1.c. adds an eighth, the Orthios
(`shrill') (cf. test. 13, fr. 2); see also Photius Lexicon
ii 26. 13 f. Naber, Suda M 1279, N 478, O 575.
<fn 3>Cf. fr. 8: fr. 2 might be an example; see also Ael. Aristid. Or. 46. 185
(ii 248 Dindorf) = 3. 231 Lenz-Behr.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>311

<TY><beg eng>20 Pollux, Vocabulary

  The divisions of the nome for cithara-singing, as
Terpander organized it, are seven in number: beginning,
after-the-beginning, downward-turn, after-the-downward-turn,
navel, seal and epilogue.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See B. A. van Groningen, Mnem. 4. 8 (1955) 177 ff.
<end fn>

<TY>21 `Plutarch', On Music

  Alexander<fn 1> in his Collected Materials on Phrygia
said ... that Terpander imitated the hexameters of
Homer but the music of Orpheus.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See Olympus test. 2.
<end fn>

<TY>22 `Plutarch', On Music

  Something must be said also about rhythms, for
new genera and species of rhythm were invented,
and indeed of melodic and rhythmic composition.
First of all, the originality of Terpander introduced a
noble style into music<fn 1>; while Polymnestus, after
the introduction of this Terpandrean style, used a
new one, although he too kept to the noble manner,
as did Thaletas and Sacadas, who were innovators at
least in rhythmic composition, but did not depart
from the noble style.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Olympus test. 7 and, for the `simple' style of T.'s
cithara-song, `Plut.' Mus. 6.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>313

<TY><beg eng>23 Plutarch, Life of Lycurgus

  This is why they say that later, when the Thebans
invaded Laconia<fn 1> and told their Helot prisoners to
sing the songs of Terpander or Alcman or Spendon
the Laconian, the Helots declined, saying that their
masters did not allow it.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>In 369 B.C.<end eng>
<end fn>

<TT><beg eng>TERPANDER

<WW>FRAGMENTS<fn 1>

<TY>1 Commentary on Aristophanes (2nd century A.D. papyrus)

<TX begin>The swan to the accompaniment of his wings
(sings a song) such as this.<TX end>

The view of Aristarchus<fn 2> is that the beginning (sc. of the
passage quoted from Aristophanes) is by Terpander,...
but it comes from the hymns ascribed to Homer [Homeric
hymn 21.1].

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Mostly of doubtful authenticity: Page accepted only fr. 1;
Wilamowitz regarded 6 and 7 as 3rd c. B.C. forgery.
<fn 2>The commentator gives alternative views of the source of
Aristophanes' words, which may have been used by more
than one poet: cf. Alcman 12B.
<end fn>

<TY>2 Suda<end eng>

  a)mfianakti/zein<fn 1>: <beg eng>to sing the nome of Terpander known as
the Orthian,<fn 2> the prelude of which began as follows:

<TX begin>About the far-shooting lord<fn 3> let my heart sing
again.<TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Comic verb used by Cratinus (fr. 72 K.-A.) and
Aristophanes (fr. 62 K.-A.) for the composition of hymnal
preludes beginning<end eng> a)mfi\... a)/nakta, <beg eng>`about the lord': see
Clouds 595. Zenobius 5.99 gives<end eng> a)lla/, a)/nac, ma/la xai=re, <beg eng>`Then
fare thee very well, lord', as the opening of the cithara-singer's

<fn 2>See testt. 13, 19 n. 2.
<fn 3>Apollo: cf. test. 2 n. 1.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>317

<TY><beg eng>3 Clement of Alexandria, Miscellanies

the solemn quality of the melody and is very ancient
provides a pattern for Terpander above all, when he sings
of Zeus to the Dorian tuning in something like these words:

<TX begin>Zeus, beginning of all, leader of all, Zeus, to you
I send this beginning of my hymns.<fn 2><TX end>

<fn 2>Rejected by Wilam. and Page as

<TY>4 Grammatical Extracts

  The spondee (--) was named after the rhythm played on
pipes and sung at<end eng> spondai/, <beg eng>`libations', e.g.

<TX begin>Let us pour libation to the Muses, the daughters
of Memory, and to the leader of the Muses, Leto's
son.<fn 1><TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Apollo. Few scholars follow Bergk in ascribing frr. 4 and 5
to Terpander.
<end fn>

<TY>5 Dionysius of Halicarnassus, On Literary Composition

  The rhythm consisting entirely of long syllables, called
`molossus' by the metricians (---), is lofty and dignified
and has a long stride, e.g.

<TX begin>(Sons) of Zeus and Leda, you handsome saviours.<fn 1><TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The Dioscuri, Castor and Polydeuces. See fr. 4 n. 1.
<end fn>

<TY>6 Strabo, Geography

  Now Arion was a cithara-singer. Terpander also, they
say, practised the same kind of music and came from the
same island (sc. Lesbos): he was the first to use the seven-stringed
instead of the four-stringed lyre, as we are told in
the hexameters attributed to him:<end eng>
<PN>319

  <beg eng>For you<fn 1> we shall make new hymns resound on
a lyre of seven notes, abandoning our love for the
four-voiced song.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Apollo? Cf. fr. 2, test. 2 n. 1.
<end fn>

<TY>7 Plutarch, Life of Lycurgus

  In short, anyone who paid attention to the poetry of
Sparta, some of which was still preserved in my time, and
examined the marching rhythms they used when going
against the enemy to pipe accompaniment, would decide
that Terpander and Pindar were quite right to associate
valour with music. The former says of Sparta,

<TX begin>There the spear of the young men flourishes and
the clear-voiced Muse and Justice who walks in
the wide streets, that helper in fine deeds.<fn 1><TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The last phrase is in Arrian only.
<end fn>

<TY>8 `Plutarch', On Music

  For they (sc. ancient cithara-singers from Terpander
onwards) first performed their duty to the gods as they
liked, then went straight on to the poetry of Homer and the
rest. This is clear from the preludes of Terpander.

<TY>9 Johannes Lydus, On the Months

  Terpander of Lesbos says Nyssa was the nurse of
Dionysus.<fn 1>

<beg fn>

<TT><beg eng>THALETAS OR THALES

<beg fn>

<fn 2>See also test. 6.

<TY>2 `Plutarch', On Music

<fn 2>Continued at test. 8.
<end fn>

<TY>3 `Plutarch', On Music

  Glaucus<fn 1> says that Thaletas was older than

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See test. 2 n. 1.
<fn 2>See test. 7.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>323

<WW><beg eng>LIFE AND WORK<fn 1>

<TY>4 `Plutarch', On Music

  One could bring forward much evidence to show
that the best regulated cities have been careful to
pay attention to noble music, in particular the cases
of Terpander, who put an end to the civil strife which
had broken out in Sparta,<fn 2> and of the Cretan Thaletas,
who is said to have arrived in Sparta in accordance
with an oracle and by means of his music
to have cured them and delivered Sparta from the
plague that gripped it, as Pratinas says.<fn 3>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also Terp. test. 22.
<fn 2>See Terp. test. 9.
<fn 3>Cf. Terp. test. 7, Philodemus Mus. i fr. 30. 23, 25, iv col. 18. 33 ff.,
19.12 ff. (he discredits the story: cf. Terp. test. 9 n. 1),
Plutarch Princ. Phil. 4: Philodemus and Plutarch both say
that Thaletas, like Terpander, put an end to civil strife in
Sparta.
<end fn>

<TY>5 Pausanias, Description of Greece

  Thales who stopped the plague for the Spartans
...: Polymnestus of Colophon, who composed
verses on Thales for the Spartans, says that he was
from Gortyn.

<TY>6 Plutarch, Life of Lycurgus

  Lycurgus through favour and friendship persuaded
one of the Cretans who had a high reputation
for wisdom and political ability to go off to Sparta:
this was Thales, who was ostensibly a composer of
songs for the lyre and used this art as a screen, but in
fact did the work of the best of the lawgivers; for his<end eng>
<PN>325

<beg eng>songs were exhortations to obedience and harmony,
composed moreover in melodies and rhythms that
were marked by great orderliness and tranquillity.
When they listened to them they became without
realising it quietened in their ways and united in
their enthusiasm for the good, giving up the mutual
ill-will that was endemic among them at the time. So
there is a sense in which Thales paved the way for
Lycurgus in his instruction of the Spartans.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Strabo 10. 4. 19, Plut. Agis 10, Aristot. Pol. 1274a. 26 ff.
(Aristotle rejects on chronological grounds the stories that
Lycurgus and Zaleucus were pupils of Thales and that
Onomacritus was his companion).
<end fn>

<TY>7 `Plutarch', On Music

  Now music was organized for the first time by
Terpander at Sparta<fn 1>; credit for the second organization
is best given to Thaletas of Gortyn, Xenodamus
of Cythera, Xenocritus of Locri, Polymnestus
of Colophon and Sacadas of Argos; for it is said that
it was on their suggestion that the festivals of the
Gymnopaediae<fn 2> at Sparta, the Apodeixeis (Exhibitions)
in Arcadia and the so-called Endymatia
(Robings) at Argos were instituted.<fn 3> Thaletas, Xenodamus
and Xenocritus were composers of paeans<fn 4>
...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Terp. test. 11.
<fn 2>Established in 668 to commemorate
Sparta's defeat by Argos at Hysiae in the previous year.
<fn 3>There are chronological difficulties over this `second
organization': if Thaletas organized the first
Gymnopaediae, he was a contemporary of Terpander, and
according to one school Polymnestus (who is later than
Thaletas: test. 5) was also as early as Terpander (Polymn.
test. 1); Xenocritus was said by Glaucus to have been
younger than Thaletas (test. 3); Sacadas did not win his
first Pythian victory till 586 (Paus. 10.7.3).
<fn 4>See test. 8.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>327

<TY><beg eng>8 `Plutarch', On Music

  Whether Thaletas of Crete composed paeans is
also disputed. For Glaucus<fn 1> ... says that Thaletas
imitated Archilochus' songs but increased their
length and also introduced into his music the
paeonic (e.g. -<*>) and cretic (-<*>-) rhythms,<fn 2>
which Archilochus had not used nor Orpheus nor
Terpander for that matter: Thaletas, they say, derived
them from the pipe music of Olympus and so
gained the reputation of an excellent poet.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See test. 2.
<fn 2>Appropriate to hyporchemata (test. 10).
<end fn>

<TY>9 Strabo, Geography (quoting Ephorus<fn 1>)

  (The Cretan lawgiver ordered) likewise that they
use in their songs the cretic rhythms, which are very
vigorous and were invented by Thales, to whom they
ascribe not only their paeans and other native songs
but also many of their institutions.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>4th c. historian (fr. 149 Jacoby).
<end fn>

<TY>10 Scholiast on Pindar

  Now some say that the dance in armour was first
invented and danced by the Curetes and that later
on Pyrrhichus the Cretan organized it again, and
that Thaletas was the first to compose the hyporchemata
(choral dance-songs) for it; and Sosibius
says all hyporchematic songs are called Cretan.<end eng>
<PN>329

<TY><beg eng>11 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Thyreatic: this is the Spartan term for certain
garlands, according to Sosibius in his treatise On
Sacrifices; ... he says that they are worn to commemorate
the victory in Thyrea (546 B.C.) by the
leaders of the choruses that are organized at that
Thyreatic festival [when they hold the Gymnopaediae
also].<fn 1> The choruses are three in number,
boys in front, old men on the right, men on the left,
dancing naked and singing songs by Thaletas and
Alcman and the paeans of Dionysodotus the
Spartan.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Jacoby struck out this clause.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>331

<TT><beg eng>POLYMNESTUS

<WW>LIFE AND WORKS

<WW>CHRONOLOGY<fn 1>

<TY>1 `Plutarch', On Music

  (Heraclides<fn 2> says also that) like Terpander<fn 3>
Clonas, who was the first to compose nomes for pipe
accompaniment and processional songs, wrote
elegiac couplets and hexameters, and that Polymnestus
of Colophon who came after him used the
same metres.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also Thal. test. 7 with n. 3.
<fn 2>See Terp. test. 18 n. 1.
<fn 3>I.e. as Terpander composed nomes for cithara
accompaniment, so Clonas (and Polymnestus) composed
them for pipe accompaniment.
<end fn>

<TY>2 `Plutarch', On Music

  Tradition has it that after Terpander and Clonas
came Archilochus. But other historians say Ardalus
of Troezen composed music for pipe accompaniment
before Clonas, and that there was also a poet Polymnestus,
son of Meles of Colophon, composer of the
nomes Polymnestus and Polymnestes<fn 1> ... Polymnestus
was mentioned by the lyric poets Pindar<fn 2>
and Alcman.<fn 3>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Text uncertain; this chronology would put P. before
Archilochus.
<fn 2>Cf. test. 7.
<fn 3>Alcman may have died c. 590.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>333

<TY><beg eng>3 `Plutarch', On Music

  Since there were three tuning-systems in the time
of Polymnestus and Sacadas,<fn 1> the Dorian, the Phrygian
and the Lydian,...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>This passage seems to make P. a contemporary of
Sacadas, whose first Pythian victory was as late as 586.
<end fn>

<WW>MUSIC AND POETRY<fn 1>

<TY>4 `Plutarch', On Music<fn 2>

  Polymnestus composed the so-called Orthian
pieces,<fn 3> Sacadas elegiacs ... Polymnestus too composed
nomes for pipe accompaniment. But whether
he used the Orthios nome<fn 4> in his melodic composition,
as the writers on harmonics<fn 5> claim, we cannot
say for certain, for the ancients<fn 6> have said
nothing about this.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also Terp. test. 22, Thal. test. 5.
<fn 2>Continued from
Thal. test. 7.
<fn 3>See Terp. test. 13 n. 3.
<fn 4>See Terp. test.
19 n. 2.
<fn 5>E.g. Archytas..
<fn 6>I.e. the lyric poets.
<end fn>

<TY>5 `Plutarch', On Music

  The nomes in the music of these poets (i.e. Clonas
and Polymnestus), my good Onesicrates, were for
pipe accompaniment: the Apothetos (`stored away
for special occasions'), Elegoi (`laments'),
Comarchius (`revel-leader's'), Schoinion (`rope'),
Cepion,<fn 1> Epicedius (`funereal') and Trimeles (`three-songed').
Later the so-called Polymnestian pieces
were invented.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See Terp. test. 19 n. 1.
<fn 2>Terp. test. 19 follows.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>335

<TY><beg eng>6 `Plutarch', On Music

  To Polymnestus is ascribed the mode now called
Hypolydian, and he is said to have greatly increased
the eklysis and ekbole.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The lowering of pitch by three quarter-tones and its
raising by five quarter-tones.
<end fn>

<TY>7 Strabo, Geography (on Colophon)

  Pindar mentions a certain Polymnestus as one of
the highly regarded figures in music: `You know the
utterance of Polymnestus, the man of Colophon, that
is available to all.'

<TY>8 Cratinus

  And he sings Polymnestian songs<fn 1> and learns
music.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Hesychius: `to sing the Polymnestian': the Polymnestian
is a kind of musical composition. Polymnestus was a lyric
poet of Colophon, very melodious (or `cheerful').
<end fn>

Aristophanes, Knights (on Ariphrades, `inventor of
cunnilinctus')

  And doing Polymnestian things<fn 1> and consorting
with Oeonichus.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Presumably parody of Cratinus in the same metre, but the
point of the joke is lost; perhaps P. and O. were both
contemporary Athenians. Later commentators seem to
have inferred wrongly that the poet P. behaved obscenely
or that his songs were obscene: schol. Ar. ad loc., schol.
Lucian (iv 235 Jacobitz), Suda on Ariphrades and
Polymnestus.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>337

<TT><beg eng>ALCMAN

<WW>LIFE AND WORKS

<WW>BIOGRAPHY

<TY>1 Suda, Alcman<fn 1>

  A Laconian from Messoa<fn 2>: Crates<fn 3> wrongly
makes him a Lydian from Sardis. A lyric poet; son of
Damas or Titarus. He was alive in the 27th Olympiad
(672/668 B.C.), when Ardys, father of Alyattes, was
King of Lydia.<fn 4> He was extremely amorous and was
the first to write amatory songs.<fn 5> His forebears were
household slaves.<fn 6> He wrote six books: lyric poetry
and the Diving Women.<fn 7> He was the first to introduce
the practice of singing poetry in rhythms other
than the hexameter.<fn 8> He used the Dorian dialect,<fn 9> as
Spartans do. There is also another Alcman,<fn 10> one of
the lyric poets, who was brought by Messene. The
plural form is `Alcmanes'.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See J. A. Davison, From Archilochus to Pindar 173-87.
<fn 2>One of the villages which comprised the town of
Sparta.
<fn 3>Crates of Mallos, flor. 168 B.C., librarian of
Pergamum; for the controversy over the birthplace see
testt. 2-9.
<fn 4>Ardys in fact ruled from c. 652 to c. 619 and
was father of Sadyattes and grandfather of Alyattes; for the
chronology see testt. 10-11, and for the synchronization of
A. and Ardys see A. A. Mosshammer, The Chronicle of
Eusebius 222-5.
<fn 5>See frr. 58 and 59(a) with the comment
of Archytas.
<fn 6>Cf. test. 12.
<fn 7>Cf. frr. 4C (n. 2), 158,
test. 16: was the Diving Women a component of the six
books, or should<end eng> me/lh <beg eng>be altered to<end eng> melw=n <beg eng>to give `six books
of lyric poetry and also the Diving Women'?
<fn 8>Haslam deletes<end eng> mh/ <beg eng>to give `in hexameter rhythms': cf. Terp.
test. 18.
<fn 9>See Page, Alcman: the Partheneion 102-63, E.
Risch in Mus. Helv. 11 (1954) 20-37.
<fn 10>See test. 29.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>339

<WW><beg eng>BIRTHPLACE<fn 1>

<TY>2 Palatine Anthology: Alexander of Aetolia<fn 2>

  Sardis, ancient dwelling-place of my fathers, if I
had been brought up in you I would have been an
acolyte carrying the offering-dish or a eunuch-priest
wearing gold ornaments, striking the noisy tambourine<fn 3>;
as it is, my name is Alcman, and I belong
to Sparta with its many tripods,<fn 4> and I have come to
know the Muses of Helicon, who have made me
greater than the despots Candaules or Gyges.<fn 5>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See test. 1, frr. 16, 24, 39 (n. 2), Terp. test. 7; also P. Oxy.
3542 (3rd c. A.D.): `some say that D(amas), his father, moved
from Lydia ..., (having) with him (his son), still a
child.'
<fn 2>The poem is quoted by Plutarch to illustrate
the possibility of a happy exile.
<fn 3>In the service of Rhea.
<fn 4>Prizes in games.
<fn 5>Lydian kings with their capital in Sardis.
<end fn>

<TY>3 Palatine Anthology: Leonidas

  Graceful Alcman, the swan who sang wedding-hymns,
<fn 1>whose song was worthy of the Muses, lies in
this tomb; he was a great delight to Sparta, where
the Lydian,<fn 2> having thrown off his burden,<fn 3> departed
to Hades.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>V.1=159 P.M.G.: frr. 4C and 107 may be from wedding-hymns;
see also M. W. Haslam, Ox. Pap. 45 (1977) 3.
<fn 2>Text uncertain: some authorities give `where he at
the end, having ...'.
<fn 3>Presumably the burden of slavery: cf. testt. 1, 12.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>341

<TY>4 <beg eng>Palatine Anthology: Antipater of Thessalonica

  Do not judge the man by the stone: his tomb is
small to look at, but it holds the bones of a great
man. You will recognize Alcman, outstanding
striker of the Spartan lyre, one of the nine,<fn 1> the
Muses' number. Here he lies, source of dispute to
two continents, whether he was Lydian or Spartan.
Singers have many mothers.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. one of the Alexandrian canon of nine lyric poets.
<end fn>

<TY>5 Velleius Paterculus, History of Rome

  Alcman is falsely aclaimed by the Spartans<fn 1> as
their own.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Perhaps an allusion to the Spartan historian Sosibius (see
fr. 94).
<end fn>

<TY>6 On the Nine Lyric Poets (quoted by Scholiast on
Pindar)

  Alcman shines strongly among the Lydians; but
his father is Damas and he is from Sparta and his
song is Dorian.

<TY>7 Scholiast B on Alcman 1.58 f. (papyrus of
50-100 A.D.)

  X<fn 1> says (the Ibenians are a people) of (Lydia), and
from this he is ready (to infer) that Alcman (was)
Lydian.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The space is too small for `Crates' (see test. 1); perhaps
`he' with reference to Aristarchus.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>343

<TY><beg eng>8 Commentary on Alcman's birthplace (same
papyrus)

  X<fn 1> would be a reliable ... authority that he (sc.
Alcman) was Spartan, when he says, `... craftsman
of skilled maiden-songs, rival to Spartan Alcman,
and fitting ... of ... songs'; but it seems that
Aristotle and (Crates?) agree in regarding him as
Lydian, deceived (by the words), `he was no rustic
man nor ...' [fr. 16].<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Some lyric poet, perhaps Pindar.
<fn 2>Similar material in P.Oxy. 2506=fr. 13(c) and (d).
<end fn>

<TY>9 Commentary on the life of Alcman and others
(papyrus of 1st or early 2nd century A.D.)

  ... the Phliasian<fn 1> ... Aeschylus ... (he) shows
that Alcman was a Spartan: for he says in his
Hyacinthia,<fn 2> `I heard the nightingales, which by (the
waters) of the Eurotas ... the Amyclaean ... best-governed
...' ... `excellence ...' somewhere in
his songs ... `... woman of Atarneus<fn 3> ...' For in
these ... to write ... Alcman ... (Xanthus?)<fn 4>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Presumably the scholar Aeschylus of Phlius mentioned
by Xen. Symp. 4. 63; but perhaps the well-known 5th c.
poets Pratinas of Phlius and Aeschylus.
<fn 2>Name of the cult of Apollo and Hyacinthus at Amyclae, south of Sparta;
it is not clear if the quotation is from Alcman himself.
<fn 3>Same mysterious word in Schol. B on Alcm.
1.60 ff.
<fn 4>5th c. Lydian historian.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>345

<beg eng>... Alcman; for he has omitted nothing else about
(Lydian) history that is in the least important.<fn 5> It is
not (surprising) that despite his Lydian origin Spartans
at that date put him in charge of the traditional
choruses of their daughters and young men ... but
did not yet (allow foreigners?) to compete, (since)
even now they still use foreigners to train their
choruses. For if the Spartans had made him a citizen
because of his poetic skill, it is (strange) that Alcman
should diffame himself in his songs and say that
he was a barbarian and a Lydian on account of his
(Lydian?) country and (race)...

<beg fn>
<fn 5>It seems that the historian of Lydia (Xanthus?) made no
mention of Alcman, an argument from silence for his non-Lydian
origin; the next sentence seems to assume his
Lydian origin, though the next again argues against it.
<end fn>

<WW>CHRONOLOGY<fn 1>

<TY>10 Eusebius, Chronicle

  (a) Olympiad 30.3 (658/657)<fn 2>: Alcmeon (i.e. Alcman)
is considered famous together with Lesches of
Lesbos, who composed the Little Iliad.

  (b) Olympiad 42.4 (609/608): according to some,
Alcman is well-known.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also test. 1.
<fn 2>Cf. A. A. Mosshammer, The

<PN>347

<TY><beg eng>11(a) Suda, Stesichorus

  Stesichorus was later than the lyric poet Alcman,
since he was born in the 37th Olympiad (632/628).<fn 1>

(b) Suda, Arion

  Arion was born in the 38th Olympiad (628/624).<fn 2>
Some said he was the pupil of Alcman.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The Suda (test. 1) gives the conventional 40-year interval
between the floruit of Alcman and the birth of
Stesichorus.
<fn 2>Eusebius' date for Arion's floruit
(Ol. 40.3 = 618/617) and his first date for Alcman's floruit
(test. 10a) give the conventional 40-year gap between pupil
and teacher.
<end fn>

<WW>ALLEGED SERVILE ORIGIN<fn 1>

<TY>12 Heraclides Lembus, On Constitutions

  Alcman was a household slave of Agesidas, but
since he was talented he was set free, and he turned
out to be a poet.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also test. 1.

<WW>DEATH

<TY>13 Aristotle, History of Animals

  Lice are produced from flesh. When they are about
to appear, a sort of small eruption forms, without
pus; if this is pricked, lice come out. Some get this<end eng>
<PN>349

<beg eng>disease when there is much moisture in the body,
and indeed people have died from it, the poet Alcman,
they say, and Pherecydes of Syros.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Cf. Plin. N.H. 11.114, Helladius in Phot. Library 533a,
where the three victims listed are the hero Acastus,
Pherecydes and Sulla. Dissolute living was thought to
aggravate phthiriasis (Plut. Sulla 36.5). O. Musso
(Prometheus 1, 1975, 183 f.) argues that the text of Aristotle
should read not `the poet Alcman' but `the physicist
Alcmaeon', as in the Excerpts of pseudo-Antigonus (Mir.
88).
<end fn>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Shrine of Sebrus, son of Hippocoon: see fr. 1.3.
<fn 2>See fr. 21.

<WW>METRES<fn 1>

<TY>17 Hephaestion, On Critical Signs

  The outward-facing diple (>)<fn 2> is common in
comic and tragic texts but rare in lyric poets. It is
found in Alcman, since he composed songs of fourteen
strophes of which the first half consisted of
seven strophes in the same metre, the other half of
strophes in a different metre. So the diple is placed
against the seven different strophes<fn 3> to indicate the
change of metre in the song.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also test. 1; for various dactylic and anapaestic
lengths labelled `Alcmanic' see 161(c) P.M.G.
<fn 2>A mark
in the left margin used e.g. to indicate a new speaker in
drama.
<fn 3>Presumably at the point where they begin.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>353

<TY><beg eng>18 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  The catalectic anapaestic tetrameter with a spondee
instead of the anapaest in the second-last foot
some call `Spartan', giving as an example, `Come,
armed youths of Sparta, to the dance of Ares' (Pop.
Song 857).

  Scholiast: since Alcman used it, and he was
Spartan.

<TY>19 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  In the archebulean<fn 1> writers other than Alcman
made the second, third and fourth feet anapaestic
like the first, thus keeping the rhythm entirely anapaestic;
but Alcman admits spondees somewhere.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Four anapaests followed by a bacchius; see e.g. M. L.
West, Greek Metre 152.
<end fn>

<TY>20 Hesychius, Lexicon

  Clepsiambi<fn 1>: according to Aristoxenus, certain
songs (i.e. metres?) in Alcman.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Used elsewhere of a type of stringed instrument. Bergk
thought that catalectic iambic trimeters (e.g. 19, 96) might
be meant.
<end fn>

<TY>21 `Plutarch', On Music

  There is also a certain originality (sc. in metre) in
Alcman and Stesichorus, although their innovations
do not abandon the noble manner<fn 1> either.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. the manner of Terpander.<end eng>
<end fn>

<WW>A SECOND ALCMAN?

<TY>29 Papyrus (150-200 A.D.): commentary on Alcman

  ... (Didymus?)<fn 1> talks nonsense in Book 3 of his
Concerning ... (when he says) that two Alcmans<fn 2>
... older ... older, the other younger ... (of) the
older ... a little ... his native land ... the times
at which he lived; Are- says of his training ...
stories ... Alcman, so that (Musaeus?)<fn 3> who lived
long before him...

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The prolific Alexandrian scholar (1st c. B.C.).
<fn 2>See end of test. 1.

<PN>361

<CT begin><beg eng>Frr. 1-13 together with 18 are papyrus finds, frr. 14-157 are

<CT begin>1-4C are papyrus fragments of the text of Alcman.<CT end>

<TY>1 The Louvre partheneion (1st c. A.D. papyrus)<fn 1>

... Polydeuces<fn 2>: I do not reckon Lycaethus<fn 3>
among the dead but Enarsphorus and swift-footed
Sebrus and ... the violent<fn 4> and ... the helmeted
and Euteiches and lord Areius and ... outstanding
among demigods;

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Found at Saqqra in 1855, first published by Egger in
1863. Grammarians and others refer to various lines of the
poem, ascribing them to Alcman: the scholiast on Pindar
and Herodian and others give garbled versions of vv. 2 and
6; Hesychius reports that Alcman gave the name Porcus to
Nereus, the sea-god (see v. 19); others discuss the words<end eng>
u(popetridi/wn <beg eng>(49),<end eng> fa/ros <beg eng>(61)<end eng>, a)mu/nasqai <beg eng>(cf. 65)<end eng>, sieidh/s <beg eng>(71).
<fn 2>Perhaps 35 lines are missing from the beginning of the
poem. A. lists the dead sons of Hippocoon, mythical king of
Sparta, who had exiled his younger brother Tyndareus,
father of Castor and Polydeuces; he was attacked by
Heracles, apparently in collaboration with Tyndareus and
his sons, and was killed together with his sons, of whom A.
names ten; Heracles established Tyndareus as king,
instructing him to hold the kingship in his family until the
return of the Heraclidae. Heracles is not mentioned in the
extant lines; but when Clement of Alexandria quotes
Sosibius to the effect that Heracles was wounded by the
sons of Hippocoon, the scholiast reports, `Alcman mentions
it in Book 1.' According to the same scholiast, Euphorion
in his Thrax made the sons of Hippocoon `rival suitors' of
Castor and Polydeuces: this may be relevant to
v. 16 ff.
<fn 3>A distant cousin of the sons of Hippocoon: see scholia A.
<fn 4>For supplementation of the missing names see Page, Alcman 27-9.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>363

<beg eng>and great ..., gatherer (of the army), and Eurytus
in the hurly-burly (of blind Ares?) and Alcon, finest
warriors, we shall by no means pass over: Fate and
Poros,<fn 5> those ancient ones, conquered them all, and
their valour which was without foundation<fn 6> collapsed.
Let no man fly to heaven or attempt to marry
Aphrodite, the (Cyprian) queen, nor some ... nor a
daughter of Porcus<fn 7> (of the sea) ...; it is the
Graces<fn 8> with love in their eyes who (frequent?) the
house of Zeus; ... god ... to friends ... gave gifts
... youth lost ... throne<fn 9> ... vain ... went; one
of them (died)<fn 10> by an arrow, (another) by a marble
millstone ... Hades ...; and unforgettably they
suffered, since they plotted evil.

<beg fn>
<fn 5>The Contriver? See fr. 5. 2 col. iii.
<fn 6>Cf. Pindar's<end eng> a)damanto-pe/dilos, <beg eng>`with foundation of adamant'.
<fn 7>Probably a Laconian sea-god (Page 1.c. 38 ff.).
<fn 8>Worshipped in Sparta along with Castor and Polydeuces;
see also fr. 62.
<fn 9>Or `time'.
<fn 10>A. seems to have moved
to a new example of hybris, probably the fighting of the
Giants against the Gods.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>365

<beg eng>There is such a thing as the vengeance of the gods:
that man is blessed who devoutly<fn 11> weaves to the
end the web of his day unweeping.

  And so I sing of the brightness of Agido: I see her
like the sun, which Agido summons to shine on us as
our witness; but our illustrious choir-leader<fn 12> by no
means allows me either to praise or to fault her<fn 13>;
for she herself seems pre-eminent, just as if one were
to put a horse among grazing herds, a sturdy,
thunderous-hoofed prize-winner, one of those seen in
rock-sheltered dreams. Why, don't you see? The
race-horse is Venetic<fn 14>; but the hair of my cousin
Hagesichora has the bloom of undefiled gold, and
her silver face -- why do I tell you openly? This is
Hagesichora here; and the second in beauty after
Agido will run like a Colaxaean horse against an
Ibenian<fn 15>; for the Pleiads,<fn 16> as we carry a plough<fn 17>

<beg fn>
<fn 11>Or `cheerfully'.
<fn 12>Presumably Hagesichora, whose
name means Choir-leader; so at 84. Agido seems to be
her principal assistant in the rites.
<fn 13>I.e. Agido.
<fn 14>From the northern Adriatic (mod. Venice); the race-horse must be Agido.
<fn 15>I.e. Agido has no close rival:
the Colaxaean horse may be from Scythia (see Bolton,
Aristeas 43), the swifter Ibenian from Lydia.
<fn 16>Subject of much controversy: probably the Pleiads (or Doves) are
a rival choir, and the imagery of fighting (63), protection
(65), guarding (77) and peace (91) refers to their competition.
Others take the Pleiads to be the star-group,
hurrying the singers at the approach of dawn or of the
ploughing-season. Others again follow the Scholiast and
take the Pleiads (or doves) to be Hagesichora and Agido;
in their view the military imagery is erotic.
<fn 17>So Sosiphanes (schol.); the word usually means `robe'.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>367

<beg eng>to Orthria,<fn 18> rise through the ambrosial night like
the star Sirius and fight against us.

  For abundance of purple is not sufficient for protection,
nor intricate snake of solid gold, no, nor Lydian
headband, pride of dark-eyed girls, nor the hair of
Nanno, nor again godlike Areta nor Thylacis and
Clesithera; nor will you<fn 19> go to Aenesimbrota's<fn 20>
and say, `If only Astaphis were mine, if only Philylla
were to look my way and Damareta and lovely
Ianthemis'; no, Hagesichora guards me.<fn 21> For is not
fair-ankled Hagesichora present here? Does she not
remain (near) Agido and commend our festival?
Come, you gods, accept their (prayers): to the gods
belong fulfilment and accomplishment. Choir-leader, -- if
I may speak -- I am myself only a girl

<beg fn>

<fn 19>Feminine.
<fn 20>Trainer of the four girls? Or a dispenser of love-potions?
<fn 21>Others read<end eng> tei/rei <beg eng>and translate, `H. wears me out (with love)'.<end eng>

<beg eng>screeching pointlessly, an owl from a rafter; but even

  For (like) the trace-horse<fn 25> ..., and in a ship too

<beg fn>

<fn 23>Not understood: perhaps a metaphorical reference to the

<fn 24>Perhaps victory in a competition, but see n. 16.
<fn 25>Presumably Hagesichora guides the choir as

<fn 26>Or `instead of' (Schol.).
<fn 27>Hagesichora again?
<fn 28>The poem ends four lines later.<end eng>

<beg eng>Scholia A (in Louvre papyrus)<fn 1>

  v. 2 the sense is this: I do not number Lycaeus with the
sons of Hippocoon ... will be not only Lycaeus but also

<beg fn>

is how they appear in the dream. He has called dreams `rock-sheltered'
since they dwell under a rock in a ... place. He is
copying Homer: cf. Odyssey [24.11f.],<fn 1> `and they went past
the streams of Ocean and the White Rock, past the gates of

<TY>2 Papyrus (50-100 A.D.)<fn 1>

`Herodian', On Figures of Speech

  The Alcmanic figure is the one which inserts plural or
dual nouns or verbs between (singular) nouns or verbs
which belong together ... This figure is used to excess in
the lyric poet Alcman,<fn 1> so that it is called Alcmanic. There
is no need to go further than the second ode<fn 2> for an
example: `Castor ... Polydeuces'.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>But this is his only extant example.
<fn 2>Presumably the second poem of Book 1.<end eng>
<end fn>

men'.

Scholiast (in the upper margin of the papyrus)

fr. 2 col. ii

  nor ... standing beside ... Aphrodite. Theon<fn 1> ... and
Tyrannion<fn 2> read<end eng> xrusw= <beg eng>(`gold') in the genitive to give the
meaning, `Nor will anyone find fault with you<fn 3> if you stand
near gold, nor will gold show you up, but you will surpass it.'

  For Calaesus is not a man of much hurt among men
nor savage: opposite is used to express opposite: Calaesus
is not `of much hurt' but prosperous,<fn 4> not savage but
civilised.

  But now let us go (trusting in the power?) of the god
as far as best<fn 5> of (his) children: Leotychidas is king of
Sparta, but it is unclear (whose) daughter Timasimbrota (is
and who is the son) and whose (son he is).

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Augustan grammarian.
<fn 2>Another grammarian, either
the Elder, Cicero's contemporary, or his pupil, the Younger.
<fn 3>Feminine.
<fn 4>This explanation is improbable; Alcman
must have meant `not hurtful nor savage'.
<fn 5>Feminine adjective.<end eng>

<PN>391

  <beg eng>In build (she) is like the yellow-haired Polydorus,
child (of Eurycrates): (Hippocratidas) is the son of
Leotychidas, king (of Sparta), but Eurycrates' (son) is
Polydorus, and Timasimbrota is his daughter.<fn 6>

  Muse, I beseech you most (among the gods): (he
invokes) the Muses on behalf of the daughter of the
-ntidae<fn 7>); and it is the tribal chorus of Dyme: -tra<fn 8> is a girl
from Dyme. In this song Alcman is cosmogonist: we shall set
out our opinions<fn 9> following the attempts of the others.
He made the Muses the daughters of Earth,<fn 10> as Mimnermus
[fr. 13 West] does.

<PN>393

<beg eng>col. iii

  ... of all ... (Thetis?).

  After that, ancient Poros and Tekmor<fn 11>: Tekmor
came into being after Poros ... thereupon ... (called
him) Poros since (the beginning `provided' all things?); for
when the matter<fn 12> began to be set in order, a certain Poros
came into being as a beginning. So Alcman (represents) the
matter of all things as confused and unformed. Then he
says that one came into being who set all things in order,
then that Poros came into being, and that when Poros had
passed by Tekmor followed. And Poros is as a beginning,
Tekmor like an end. When Thetis<fn 13> had come into being, a
beginning and end of all things came into being
(simultaneously), and all things<fn 14> have their nature
resembling the matter of bronze, while Thetis has hers
resembling that of the craftsman, Poros and Tekmor
resembling the beginning and the end. He uses the word
`ancient'<end eng> [1pre/sgus]1 <beg eng>for `old'.

  And the third, darkness: since neither sun nor moon
had come into being yet, but matter was still
undifferentiated. So (at the same moment?) there came into
being Poros and Tekmor and darkness.<fn 15>

<beg fn>
<fn 11>See J. L. Penwill, Apeiron 8 (1974) 13 ff.: Poros,
the Contriver at 1.14, may here be rather the `Passage'
created between Heaven and Earth; Tekmor is probably
`Ordinance'.
<fn 12>The commentator uses an Aristotelian
term in his explanation: so with `beginning' and `end'.
<fn 13>Not the sea-goddess, but `Creation' (=Attic<end eng> qe/sis: <beg eng>cf.
20. 1<end eng> e)/shke, <beg eng>probably from this poem).
<fn 14>By `all things'
he means the<end eng> u(/lh, <beg eng>`matter'.
<fn 15>This explanation seems
wrong-headed: darkness is third not to Poros and Tekmor
but to `day and moon', as is shown by the longer quotation
which follows.<end eng>
<end fn>

  <beg eng>Day and moon and the third, darkness (as far as)
flashing(s)<fn 16>: `day' does not mean simply `day', but
contains the idea of the sun. Previously there was only
darkness, and afterwards, when it had been differentiated,
(light came into being).

<TY>14 Syrianus on Hermogenes, On Kinds of Style

  Now the strophe, the period which stands first,<fn 1> consists
of two or more cola (metrical phrases), which may be
similar or dissimilar: similar as in Alcman [fr. 27],
dissimilar as in this example:

  (a) Come, Muse, clear-voiced Muse of many songs,
singer always, begin a new<fn 2> song for girls to sing.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. before antistrophe and epode.
<fn 2>According to Erotianus Alcman uses the adjective in book 1. The newness
may lie in the metre.
<end fn>

Priscian, On the Metres of Terence

  In their iambic lines Simonides and Alcman, according
to Heliodorus, place a spondee not only at the end but in
other positions also: Simonides [fr. 533]; Alcman in book 1
composed catalectic trimeters with sometimes an iamb,
sometimes a spondee in the fourth position, as follows:

  (a) = begin a new (song) for girls to sing<fn 1>;

  (b) and the holy temple of well-towered Therapne,<fn 2>
which has a spondee in the fourth position; so in

  (c) (the wave?) falls mutely on the land<fn 3> among the
seaweed,

he put a spondee in the fourth position -- for the first
syllable of<end eng> fu/kessi <beg eng>is long -- according to Heliodorus, who
says Simonides often does this.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Regular iambic trimeter catalectic.
<fn 2>See fr. 7 and Harpocration cited there.
<fn 3>If all the lines come from
the same poem, the beach may be that of Pephnos, where
the Dioscuri were said to have been born (fr. 23).<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>409

<TY><beg eng>15 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  This (sc. the lengthening of syllables `by position') occurs
in five different ways: either the syllable will end in two
consonants, as with `Tiryns', [anon. fragment] or<end eng> ma/kars,<fn 1>
<beg eng>`blessed':

<TX begin>and he sitting, blessed man,<fn 2> amid much good
cheer ...<TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Apollonius Dyscolus says Alcman used<end eng> ma/kars e)kei=nos,
<beg eng>`blessed he', in book 1.
<fn 2>Perhaps of the deified Heracles.
<end fn>

<TY>16 Stephanus of Byzantium, Lexicon of Place-names

  Erysiche: a city of Acarnania, later named Oeniadae ...
The ethnic adjective is<end eng> E)rusixai=os, <beg eng>`Erysichaean', about
which the ancients had much to say: the grammarian
Herodian says that<end eng> E)rusi/xaios, <beg eng>so accented, is marked in
texts as being an exception among ethnic adjectives, so
perhaps, he says, it is made up of<end eng> xai=on, <beg eng>`a cowman's stick',
and the future of<end eng> e)ru/w, <beg eng>`drag': it will therefore be
ambiguous, as is evident in Alcman at the beginning of
book 2 of the partheneia, for he says,

  he was no rustic man nor clumsy (not even in the
view of unskilled men?) nor Thessalian by race nor
an Erysichaean shepherd: he was from lofty Sardis.<fn 1>

For if the adjective is to be linked with `Thessalian by
race', it is an ethnic adjective and should be accented<end eng>
E)rusixai=os, <beg eng>as Herodian in his Universal Prosody and
Ptolemaeus said; but if it is linked with `nor a shepherd',
i.e. `nor a shepherd or erysichaean', clearly it will be
accented<end eng> e)rusi/xaios <beg eng>and indicates `cowman' or `goatherd',
next to which the word `shepherd' will be aptly introduced.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>This is the passage which was taken, rightly or wrongly,
to prove the Lydian origin of Alcman: see test. 8, fr.
13(c)(d).<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>411

<TY><beg eng>17 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Even the poet Alcman puts his gluttony on record in
these lines from book 3:

  and some day I shall give you a great tripod bowl,<fn 1>
in which you may collect (provisions packed together?).
It has still not been over a fire, but soon it
will be full of pea-soup, the kind that Alcman, who
eats everything, loves hot after the solstice: he eats
no (sweet confections?) but looks for common fare
like the people.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>A prize he hopes to win and present to the choir or choirleader?
<fn 2>Continued at fr. 20.
<end fn>

<TY>21 Pausanias, Description of Greece

  ... but who wrote that Timalcus, the son of Megareus,
went to Aphidna with the Dioscuri<fn 1>? And how could he be
thought to have been killed on his arrival by Theseus,
when Alcman in a song he composed to the Dioscuri tells
how they conquered Athens and carried off Theseus'
mother as their prisoner but says that Theseus himself was
absent.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Pausanias is expressing his disagreement with a story
told in Megara. When Theseus kidnapped Helen, her
brothers the Dioscuri went to Aphidna in N.E. Attica and
carried off Theseus' mother in reprisal.
<end fn>

Scholiast on Homer, Iliad 3.242

  Helen, carried off by Paris, has no knowledge of the
disaster that has overtaken her brothers the Dioscuri in
the meantime, but imagines that they have not come to
Troy because they are ashamed of her: she had previously
been carried off by Theseus, as has been said already. On
account of that incident Aphidna, a city in Attica, was
sacked and Castor was wounded in the right thigh by
Aphidnus, king at that time. The Dioscuri having failed to
get hold of Theseus plundered Athens. The story is in
(Polemon?) or the cyclic poems and in part in the lyric poet
Alcman.<end eng>

<TY>23 Pausanias, Description of Greece

  Two-and-a-half miles from Thalamae is a place on the
coast called Pephnos.<fn 1> Off it lies an island no bigger than a
big rock, and it too is called Pephnos. The people of
Thalamae say the Dioscuri were born there. I know that
Alcman said so in a song; but they say<fn 2> that they were
brought up not on Pephnos but at Pellana,<fn 3> and that it was
Hermes who took them there.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>On the shore of the gulf of Messene.
<fn 2>Perhaps read `he says'.
<fn 3>16 miles N.W. of Sparta.

<TY>24 Himerius, Oration

  Alcman, who mingled the Dorian lyre with Lydian songs,
happened to be carrying songs through Sparta to the
temple of Zeus Lycaeus<fn 1>; but he did not pass Sparta before
greeting both the city itself and the Dioscuri.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>In Arcadia.

<TY>25 Scholiast on Virgil, Georgics (`such was Cyllarus,
subdued by the reins of Amyclaean Pollux')

  Amycla is a city in the Peloponnese; the horses given to
Juno by Neptune are called Cyllarus and Xanthus by the
lyric poet Alcman: Cyllarus was said to have been given<fn 1>
to Pollux, Xanthus to his brother; for Cyllarus belonged to
Pollux.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>By Juno, according to another scholiast on the passage,
who adds, `as unimportant Greek poets tell'; cf. frr. 2, 76,
Stes. 178.<end eng>

<TY>26 Antigonus of Carystus, Marvels

  Male halcyons are called ceryli.<fn 1> When they become
weak from old age and are no longer able to fly, the females
carry them, taking them on their wings.<fn 2> What Alcman
says is connected with this: weak from old age and unable
to whirl about with the choirs and the girls' dancing, he
says,

  No longer, honey-toned, strong-voiced<fn 3> girls, can
my limbs carry me.<fn 4> If only, if only I were a cerylus,
who flies along with the halcyons over the flower of
the wave<fn 5> with resolute heart, strong,<fn 6> sea-blue
bird.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Both mythical seabirds, sometimes identified with the
kingfisher.
<fn 2>This marvel is contradicted by Alcman's text.
<fn 3>Or `holy-voiced'.
<fn 4>The lines may be part of a solo hexameter prelude to a choral
song: cf. Terp. test. 19.
<fn 5>Aristophanes adapts this line in Birds 250 f.
<fn 6>Perhaps `holy': cf. Simon. 508. 4 ff.; probably not `sea-blue
bird of Spring'.
<end fn>

<TY>27 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

  Alcman put whole strophes in this metre (viz. dactylic
tetrameter acatalectic)<fn 1>:

  Come, Muse, Calliope, daughter of Zeus, begin the
lovely verses; set desire on the song and make the
choral dance graceful.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See also fr. 14.<end eng>
<end fn>

<TY>28 <beg eng>Scholiast on Homer

  The ending<end eng> -fi <beg eng>is used by Homer in three cases, genitive,
dative and accusative...; Alcman the lyric poet has it in
the vocative, thus:

  Muse, daughter of Zeus, I shall sing clearly,
(heavenly one?).<fn 1>

For<end eng> w)rani/afi <beg eng>is<end eng> ou)rani/a, <beg eng>`heavenly one'.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Most modern scholars reject the vocative form.
<end fn>

<TY>29 Achilles, Commentary on Aratus (`From Zeus let us
begin')

  They find fault with Aratus for using the preposition<end eng> e)k
<beg eng>instead of<end eng> a)po/ <beg eng>(`from'); but they are ignorant of the fact that
Pindar too has this usage [Nem. 2. 1-3], and Alcman:

<TX begin>and I shall sing beginning<fn 1> from Zeus.<TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Feminine participle: a girl or girls' choir is singing.
<end fn>

<TY>30 Aelius Aristides, On a Remark made in Passing

  And you hear the Spartan saying to himself and the
choir:

  The Muse cries out, that clear-voiced Siren.

... And this point too, that the poet, having in the first
place requested the Muse herself, so that he might become
active under her influence, goes on to say as though he has
changed his mind that the choir itself instead of the Muse
has become what he says.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>I.e. that his choir has been his source of inspiration.
<end fn>

<TY>34 Scholiast on Theocritus 12

  The idyll is entitled<end eng> A)i/ths <beg eng>and is written in the Ionic
dialect. The speech is addressed by the poet in his own
name to a beloved boy, whence the title<end eng> A)i/ths, <beg eng>since some
people, the Thessalians for example, call a beloved boy an<end eng>
a)i/ths. <beg eng>Alcman uses<end eng> a)i/ties <beg eng>for

<TX begin>darling girls.<fn 1><TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>C. Gallavotti, Q.U.C.C. 27 (1978) 183 ff. argues that the
scholiast wrote<end eng> xorda/s, <beg eng>not<end eng> ko/ras, <beg eng>so that Alcman sang of his
`darling lyre-strings'.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>423

<TY>35 <beg eng>Etymologicum Magnum<end eng>

  kala/ <beg eng>(`beautifully'): the form in Alcman is<end eng> ka/lla, <beg eng>e.g.

<TX begin>singing<fn 1> beautifully.<TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Feminine plural participle, used of a girls' choir.
<end fn>

<TY>36 Apollonius Dyscolus, Pronouns

  The form<end eng> a(me/s <beg eng>(`we') is Doric, as in Alcman:

<TX begin>as we (sing?) the beautiful song.<TX end>

And the accent on<end eng> a(me/s <beg eng>is quite correct.

<TY>37 Apollonius Dyscolus, Pronouns

  Doric<end eng> a(mi/n (= h(mi=n, <beg eng>`to us') shortens the<end eng> i <beg eng>when it is enclitic
and has the circumflex on the first syllable<end eng> [1a(=min]1, <beg eng>as in

  (a) If only these were of interest to us!
and when it has the acute on the last syllable<end eng> [1a(mi/n]1, <beg eng>as in

  (b) and to our song he will pipe an accompaniment.

So Alcman.

<TY>38 Apollonius Dyscolus, Pronouns

  The form<end eng> a(mw=n <beg eng>in Doric signifies a possessive genitive (`of
our') that corresponds to<end eng> a(mo/s <beg eng>(`our'). The original pronoun
(`of us') differs from the possessive in its diaeresis<end eng> [1a(me/wn]1
<beg eng>and does not admit the same form<end eng> (a(mw=n). <beg eng>So Alcman:

  and all the girls among us praise the lyre-player.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Alcman himself?<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>425

<TY>39 <beg eng>Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Some writers call partridges caccbae, as does Alcman
when he says,

  These words and melody Alcman invented by observing
<fn 1> the tongued cry of partridges (caccabdes).<fn 2>
He makes it clear that he learned to sing from the
partridges. That is why Chamaeleon of Pontus said that the
invention of music was devised by the ancients from the
birds singing in lonely places.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Perhaps `by organising', i.e. by putting into words; see B.
Gentili, Studi... in onore di Vittorio de Falco, Naples,
1971, 59-67.
<fn 2>The species in question is the chukar
partridge (Alectoris chukar), which calls kakkabi; since the
bird is found in Asia Minor and the eastern Aegean islands
but not to the west, the passage may be evidence that
Alcman grew up in Lydia, not in Sparta: see K. Borthwick
ap. W. G. Arnott, C.Q. 27 (1977) 337 n. 1.
<end fn>

<TY>40 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  The Doric form of the word<end eng> o)/rnis, <beg eng>`bird', is<end eng> o)/rnic, <beg eng>genitive<end eng>
o)/rnixos. <beg eng>But Alcman shows the nominative<end eng> o)/rnis <beg eng>(fr. 26. 4)
and the genitive plural<end eng> o)rni/xwn:

  <beg eng>and I know the tunes of all birds.

as the Spartan poet has said.<end eng>

<TY>50 Hephaestion, Handbook on Metres

other two are pure ionic, <*><*>--: e.g.

  (b) Ino, queen of the sea, whom<fn 2> from her breast
...

<beg fn>

<beg eng>Menander, On Display Oratory

  Hymns of invocation are longer in poetry, for poets can
mention many locations, as we often find in Sappho and
Alcman: he(?) summons Artemis from thousands of
mountains and cities, from rivers too, and she(?) summons
Aphrodite from Cyprus, Cnidos, Syria and many other
places.<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Text doubtful: see Russell-Wilson ad loc.: the distinction
made between Sappho and Alcman is uncertain, and it is
possible that `Alcman' should be replaced by `Anacreon';
cf. Sa. test. 47.

<TY>56 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Asclepiades of Myrlea in his work On Nestor's Cup says
that the<end eng> sku/fos <beg eng>and<end eng> kissu/bion <beg eng>(types of cup) were used not by
city-dwellers and well-to-do people but by swineherds,
shepherds and country-dwellers like Eumaeus (Od.
14. 112 f.). Alcman also says,

  Often among the mountain-peaks, when the festival
with its many torches gives pleasure to the gods,
you<fn 1> held a golden vessel, a great cup<end eng> (sku/fos), <beg eng>such
as shepherds hold, and putting into it with your
hands the milk of a lioness you made a great firm
cheese for the Slayer of Argus.<fn 2>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Addressed to a Bacchant, a female votary of Dionysus.
According to Aristides, `a Spartan poet' attributed to
Dionysus the power of milking lionesses.
<fn 2>Hermes,
who was represented on the throne of Apollo at Amyclae
carrying the child Dionysus to heaven (Paus. 3. 18. 11).<end eng>

<TY><beg eng>57 Plutarch, Table-Talk

...<fn 1>:

<TY>59 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

again pours sweetly down and warms my heart.

<TY><beg eng>62 Pausanias, Description of Greece

  On the way down to Amyclae from Sparta there is a river
called the Tiasa; they believe that Tiasa was a daughter of
Eurotas. Near the river is a sanctuary of the Graces,
Phaenna and Cleta, as Alcman said in his poetry. They
think it was Lacedaemon who established the sanctuary
there for the Graces and gave them their names.

<TY>63 Scholiast on Iliad 6. 21 (`nymph')

<TX begin>Naiads<fn 1> and Lampads and Thyiads,<TX end>

Thyiads being those who revel and go wild, i.e. go out of
their minds, with Dionysus, Lampads those who carry
torches and lights with Hecate.

<beg fn>

and daughter of Foresight,

as in Alcman's account of her lineage.<end eng>

<TY><beg eng>65 Scholiast on Iliad 1. 222 (`to join the other gods')

  Homer calls the gods<end eng> dai/mones <beg eng>like this either because
they are knowledgeable<end eng> [1dah/mones]1 ... <beg eng>or because they are
arbitrators<end eng> [1diathtai/]1 <beg eng>and controllers of men, as Alcman

[1daimona/s]1,

<beg eng>i.e. their apportionments or shares.

<TY>66 Tzetzes on Iliad 1. 4 (`heroes')

  Thales, Pythagoras, Plato and the Stoics know of a
distinction between daimones (gods, spirits) and heroes
(demigods). Daimones, they say, are bodiless beings,
whereas heroes are souls separated from bodies. Orpheus,
Homer, Hesiod, Alcman the lyric poet and the rest of the
poets show no regularity in their acceptance of all this.

<TY>67 Diodorus Siculus, World History

  Most of the mythographers, including those of the
highest reputation, say that the Muses are the daughters of
Zeus and Mnemosyne (Memory); but one or two of the
poets, Alcman among them, make them the daughters of
Uranus and Ge (Heaven and Earth).<fn 1>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>See fr. 5 col. ii (end) with n. 10.<end eng>

<TY><beg eng>85 Apollonius Dyscolus, Pronouns

  Very many usages (sc. of the possessive adjective) can be
found in other writers:<end eng> sfe/teron pate/ra <beg eng>(Hes. Op. 2) for `your
father',<end eng> e(o/s <beg eng>for<end eng> teo/s, <beg eng>`your', in Callimachus (fr. 472 Pfeiffer),
`tell your anxieties', and again in him<end eng> teo/s <beg eng>for<end eng> sfwi/teros
<beg eng>(`your', dual). Alcman<fn 1> has

<TX begin>(a) both you and your horses,<fn 2>
(b) and I fall at your knees.<fn 3><TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Both of Alcman's adjectives are probably dual, although
that in (b) may be singular.
<fn 2>Addressed to the Dioscuri? Cf. frr. 2, 25.
<fn 3>Perhaps addressed to Arete
(and Alcinous?): cf. Od. 7. 146 f., where Odysseus says,
`Arete ..., I come to your husband and to your knees,
having suffered much.'
<end fn>

<TY>86 Homeric Parsings (on Od. 3. 71<end eng> plei=te, <beg eng>`you sail')

  And<end eng> plh=tron <beg eng>is a term meaning<end eng> phda/lion; <beg eng>Alcman used the
diminutive form<end eng> plh/qrion,

<TX begin><beg eng>steering-paddle.<TX end>

<TY>87 Apollonius Dyscolus, Syntax

  For time and time again Doric<fn 1> runs syllables together
by using the corresponding unaspirated consonant (i.e.<end eng> k t
p, <beg eng>not<end eng> x q f):

  <beg eng>(a) and the bowman Heracles

  (b) to accompany most beautifully on the pipes

  (c) and mighty Athena

  (d) and Melampus and Harpolycus<fn 2>

  (e) for (the bolder would rule?)

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The ms. has `Alcman's' in the margin opposite the
examples.
<fn 2>Melampus was a famous seer; but Hyginus
fab. 181 names Melampus and Harpalicus (Blackfoot and
Snatcher) as two of the hounds of Actaeon.<end eng>
<end fn>

<TY>88<beg eng> Apollonius Dyscolus, Pronouns

  Again, Alcman used<end eng> sfea=s <beg eng>for the singular (`her', not

<TY>89 Apollonius the Sophist, Homeric Lexicon (on<end eng> knw/dalon)

  <beg eng>Homer uses the word once only (Od. 17. 317) and means
by it any wild animal; others use<end eng> qh=res <beg eng>and<end eng> qhri/a <beg eng>(`wild
animals') for lions, leopards, wolves and suchlike,<end eng> e(rpeta/
<beg eng>(`creeping things') as a general term for the different kinds
of snakes,<end eng> knw/dala <beg eng>for sea-monsters, whales and the like.
This is the distinction made by Alcman when he says,

  And the mountain-peaks are asleep and the
ravines, the headlands and the torrent-beds, all the
creeping tribes<fn 1> that the black earth nourishes, the
wild animals of the mountains, the race of bees and
the monsters in the depths of the surging sea; and
the tribes of long-winged birds are asleep.<fn 2><end eng>

<beg fn>
<beg eng><fn 1>Or `... the torrent-beds, the forest and all the creeping
things'.
<fn 2>The continuation may have been, `but X is not
asleep'; or Alcman may be setting the scene for the
epiphany of a god.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>457

<TY><beg eng>90 Scholiast on Sophocles, Oedipus at Colonus 1248 (`from
night-wrapped Rhipae')

  He is speaking of the mountains called Rhipae: some in
fact call them `the Rhipaean mountains'. He speaks of them
as `night-wrapped' because they are situated in the west.<fn 1>
Alcman mentions them in these words:

  Rhipae, mountain blossoming with forest, breast
of black night.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Sophocles clearly thinks of them as in the north, as do
other Greek and Latin writers: see Bolton, Aristeas 39 ff.
with n. 4.
<end fn>

<CT begin>91-101 deal with feasting, food and wine; cf. 17, 19.<CT end>

  wearing<fn 2> a golden chain (like?) petals of slender
calchae.<fn 3>

<fn 2>The participle is masc. sing.
<fn 3>I.e., as bright as petals ...? Text and translation uncertain.

<TY>92 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Alcman somewhere uses the expressions

<TX begin>(a) wine unfired<TX end>

and

<TX begin>(b) smelling of flowers<TX end>

of the wine from

<TX begin>(c) Five Crests,<TX end>

a place just under a mile from Sparta, and of the wines from
Denthiades, a fortress, Carystus near the Arcadian border,<end eng>
<PN>459

<beg eng>Oenus, Onogli and Stathmi: these places<fn 1> are near Pitana.
He says, then,

<TX begin>(d) and wine of Oenus or Denthian or Carystian or
Onoglian or Stathmite.<TX end>

By `unfired' he meant `not mulled' -- they used to mull their
wines.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The last three? None of the sites is identified, but
Denthiades is probably Dentheliades on the Messenian
frontier, and the river Oenus joins the Alpheus from the
N.E. just N. of Sparta.
<end fn>

<TY>93 Ammonius, On Similar but Different Words<end eng>

<TY>94 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  The cakes called thridakiskai by Alcman are the same as
the Attic thridakinai (`lettuce-cakes'). Alcman speaks of

<TX begin>lettuce-cakes and pan-cakes.<TX end><end eng>
<PN>461

<beg eng>Sosibius<fn 1> in book 3 of his work On Alcman says kribanai
(`pan-cakes') is the name for certain cakes shaped like
breasts.

  Apollodorus<fn 2> says that kribanai is a special name for
cakes in Alcman; similarly Sosibius in book 3 of his work
On Alcman, who says they are shaped like breasts and used
by Spartans for their women's festivals; he says they carry
them round when the followers in the choir are going to
sing the song of praise prepared in honour of the Maiden.<fn 3>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>3rd C.B.C. Spartan historian.
<fn 2>2nd C.B.C. Athenian scholar.
<fn 3>Artemis?
<end fn>

<TY>95 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Moreover, Polemon<fn 1> says that the evening meal is called
aiklon by the Spartans, and that all Dorians alike use this
name. Alcman says,

  (a) (mourns?)<fn 2> both at the mill and at the communal
suppers,

using the term syn-aiklia for the shared evening meals. And
again,

  (b) Alcman prepared a supper for himself.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Early 2nd C.B.C. antiquarian from Ilium.
<fn 2>`Is mourned?' Sense much disputed: see L. Massa Positano,
P.P. 1 (1946) 367-9.<end eng>
<end fn>
<PN>463

<TY><beg eng>96 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Porridge is mentioned by Alcman in these words:

  Soon he<fn 1> will provide bean porridge and white
frumenty and the waxen harvest.<fn 2>
Puanion,<fn 3> according to Sosibius,<fn 4> is mixed seeds boiled in
raisin-syrup. `Frumenty' is boiled wheaten-grains. By
`waxen harvest' he means honey.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Some link the lines with fr. 20 and take Zeus to be the
subject; Wilamowitz suggested that 96 and 19 belong to the
same poem.
<fn 2>Or `wax-like fruit'.
<fn 3>An adjective in
Alcman, it should mean `bean' porridge, according to
Hesychius. The mixed seeds may include peas, lentils,
lupins, vetch; see P. Cartledge, Sparta and Lakonia 175.
<fn 4>See 94 n. 1.
<end fn>

<TY><beg eng>100 Athenaeus, Scholars at Dinner

  Hermon<fn 1> in his Cretan Glossary says that kodymala is a
name for quinces. Polemon<fn 2> in book 5 of his Reply to
Timaeus says that according to some writers the
kodymalon is a kind of flower. Alcman uses the term of the
struthium<fn 3> when he says,

<TX begin>smaller than a medlar.<TX end>

Apollodorus and Sosibius<fn 4> take it to mean quince here, but
Theophrastus in book 2 of his Enquiry into Plants (2. 2. 5)
says clearly that the quince is different from the struthium.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Or Hermonax, unknown; see R.E. s.v. Hermonax (3).
<fn 2>See 95 n. 1.
<fn 3>Theophrastus implies that the quince
(Cydonian apple) is an inferior kind of struthium, which
may be a medlar.
<fn 4>See 94 nn. 1, 2.

<TY><beg eng>107 Aelius Aristides, In Defence of Oratory

  And what does the praiser and counsellor of the girls, the
Spartan poet, have to say?

  Say-much is the man's name, Happy-with-all the
woman's.<fn 1>

He means, `let the man say much and the woman be happy
with whatever she hears.'

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Regarded by some as part of a wedding-hymn; if so,
satirical: the woman's name, like Pasiphile in [Archilochus]
331 West, suggests promiscuity, so the man's may
be Protest-much (Garzya) or even Collect-much (McKay,
Mnem. 27, 1974, 413 f.).
<end fn>

<TX begin>the neighbourhood is briny.<TX end>

He meant, `It is a bad thing to have the sea as a neighbour.'<fn 1>

briny neighbourhood', and says that Alcman used the

<TY><beg eng>121 Priscian, Grammar (on digamma)

  They are found using this digamma to give a shortened
vowel, as in Alcman:

<TX begin>and storm and destructive fire.<fn 1><TX end>

For the line is iambic diameter, and the digamma must be
introduced in such a way as to create a short syllable<end eng> [1*i]1.<fn 2>

<beg fn>

<fn 2>Elsewhere he says that the digamma is introduced to avoid
hiatus.

<TY>122 Scholiast on Iliad 12.66<end eng> [1stei=nos, <beg eng>`narrow place')

  The word<end eng> stei=nos <beg eng>must have the accent on the first
syllable, for it has been used as a neuter noun (from adjective<end eng>
steino/s, <beg eng>`narrow'), and all neuter nouns ending in<end eng> -os
<beg eng>are accented before the last syllable. So we give<end eng> makro/s,
<beg eng>masculine adjective, an acute accent on the last syllable,
but when it becomes the neuter noun the first syllable is
accented<end eng> [1to\ ma/kros]1. <beg eng>So with<end eng> kleito/s, <beg eng>`famous': when it
becomes a neuter noun,<end eng> to\ klei=tos, <beg eng>`fame', the first syllabel is
accented in Alcman:

<TX begin>(Thessalian?) fame.<fn 1><TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Text uncertain. Cf. 16.3 for the insulting force of
`Thessalian'.
<end fn>

<TY>123 Scholiast on Iliad 22. 305

  `but having done a great thing': the word `good' is
ommitted. So in Alcman:

<TX begin>Neighbour is a great thing for neighbour.<end eng><TX end>
<PN>481

<TY><beg eng>124 Scholiast on Odyssey 3. 171

  Psyra is a small islet ten miles off Chios with a harbour
for twenty ships. Alcman has

  taking Dionysus to the holy rock, to Psyra,<fn 1>

in other words, considering him of no account, since the
island is a wretched place.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>It is not certain that the words `taking Dionysus' belong
to Alcman; Stephanus of Byzantium cited the phrase
`taking Dionysus to Psyra' from the comic poet Cratinus
(fr. 347 K.-A.). A proverb, `Dionysus to Psyra', was said
(with strange logic) to be applicable to people not drinking
at a party, since Psyra could not produce wine.
<end fn>

<TY>148 Aelius Aristides, On a Remark made in Passing<fn 1>

  Elsewhere when Alcman brags about the number of
people among whom he has a high reputation, he lists so
many and such obscure races that the wretched school-teachers
are still trying to discover where on earth they
are; they would do better to go on a long journey, if you ask
me, instead of worrying pointlessly about those

<TX begin>Shadow-foots.<fn 2><TX end>

<beg fn>
<fn 1>Followed by fr. 106.
<fn 2>Cf. scholiast on Ar. Birds 1553:
they went on all fours and raised one gigantic foot to
provide shelter from the sun in their flat country.
Stephanus of Byzantium calls them an Ethiopian race,
citing Hecataeus, Journey round Egypt.
<end fn>

Strabo, Geography

  But no one could accuse Hesiod of ignorance<fn 1> when he
mentions the Half-dogs and Long-heads and Pygmies
(fr. 153 M.-W.), nor Homer himself when he tells these tales,
including the one about the Pygmies (Il. 3.2 ff.), nor
Alcman when he talks of the

<TX begin>Umbrella-foots,<TX end>

nor Aeschylus with his Dog-heads, Chest-eyes and One-eyes.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>As did Apollodorus, On the Catalogue of Ships, citing
Eratosthenes with approval (Strabo 7.3.6).<end eng>

<TY>162 Papyrus (late 2nd or 3rd c. A.D.)<fn 1>

fr. 1(a) ... (companions) of the same age ...
wealthy (girls?) ...

(b) ... look about ... (were?) immediately returning
... (to? from? at?) the base ... near ...
beautiful(ly) ... shining ... (they) were going ...

fr. 2 ... crimson ... hair ... hairbands ... and
beautiful ... full of perfume ... put tightly-woven
willows on ... ivory ... adornments ... young
girls ... feet ... down from the head ... water
... long-winged ...

difficulties see Page, C.R. 73 (1959) 21. The scholiast at 1(a)
i(a) 5 suggests that some conclusion is being drawn from

45, p. 17f.<end eng>

<TY><beg eng>163 Athenagoras, Embassy for the Christians

  The Athenians have established Celeus and Metanira as
gods, ... the Samians Lysander despite all his murders
and crimes, Alcman and Hesiod<fn 1> Medea, the Cilicians
Niobe, ... the Carthaginians Hamilcar.

<beg fn>
<fn 1>The poets' names are unexpected in a list of ten peoples;
but Hesiod regards Medea as immortal (Theog. 992).

Alcman<fn 1> mentions the proverb.

Sappho or Alcaeus 15 (vol. i, p. 445).

<TY><beg eng>168, 169 Apollonius Dyscolus, Pronouns

  For when the pronoun<end eng> tu/, <beg eng>`you', has the acute accent, it is

<TX begin>and you, mighty daughter of Zeus (169);<TX end>

<TX begin>and made you a horse-lover (168).<TX end>
